==PAGE:7==
Abstract
Occupant behaviour has a large effect on the indoor climate and is also an essential factor when
predicting energy consumption. Especially window openings affect the indoor climate and energy
use [37]. Few models explaining occupant behaviour in regards to window operation in dwellings
exist whereas most solely are based on thermal indoor and outdoor environmental conditions [2].
Discrepancies between predicted energy use and actual energy use are therefore often found [19]
and trigger a need for better and more precise occupant behavioural models.
The aim of this thesis was to model occupants’ window operation behaviour in residential buildings.
During a period of 9-12 months, indoor climate, outdoor climate, window movements, and presence
related measurements were conducted in 37 townhouses located north of Copenhagen. A total of
179 windows were monitored in several room types. The window operations were modelled based
on the climate measurements as well as the contextual and time related explanatory variables
room type, season, time of day, and presence status. Furthermore, the models were only based on
data when presence was detected in the dwellings. Diversity of occupant behaviour was included
by deriving window operation models for each dwelling. Three types of models were derived; 1)
a model describing the window opening probability, 2) a model describing the window closing
probability, and 3) a model predicting the angle of a window opening. Model type 1 and 2 were
conducted as stochastic generalized linear models using multivariate logistic regression. Model
type 3 was derived using multivariate basic linear regression.
The models showed that there were different behavioural patterns in each dwelling. However,
some patterns were more common than others. Generally, the two most included variables in
all model types were season and room type. However, the most influential variables differed.
Towards the window opening probability, they were season, indoor temperature, and outdoor
temperature. In regards to window closing probabilities, they were indoor temperature, season,
indoor relative humidity, and outdoor temperature. Lastly, the most influential variables towards
predicting opening angles were room type, outdoor temperature, and season.
Additionally, a
nearly exponential correlation between opening angles and opening durations was found where
angles decreased with increasing durations.
After validations, the derived window operation models could be used in energy simulations. The
models were based on actual data and may explain the actual energy use better than the current
methods. However, the models could still benefit from further exploration.

1
Introduction
Nowadays, the building industry is responsible for 40% of the total energy consumption in EU
[16]. Among the world wide interest of decreasing the energy use, the built sector is therefore
worth investigating.
In order to reduce the energy consumption of buildings, new initiatives are needed. This requires
a deep understanding of how the energy performance is influenced. Today, it is well known that
occupant behaviour has a large effect on the energy consumption [37]. Actually, a gap between
the estimated and actual energy use has been found [19] which is assumed to be due to occupant
behaviour. During the last decades, there has been an increased focus on remedying this energy
gap by trying to understand and model occupant behaviour more realistically. Despite this, and
due to the complexity of understanding and modelling occupant behaviour, a lack of occupant
behavioural models still exists.
Based on more than 25 years of studies on occupant behaviour, window openings were found to be
one of the most common actions performed by occupants when adjusting the indoor environment
[21], [37].
The aim of this master thesis was to model occupant behaviour in regards to window operations
1 of 253
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 1 | Introduction
in residential buildings and include the stochasticity which lies behind human behaviour. The
model types derived in this thesis and the corresponding mathematical approach were as follows:
• Window opening model; generalized linear model with multivariate logistic regression
• Window closing model; generalized linear model with multivariate logistic regression
• Opening angle model; multivariate basic linear model
The models were based on measurements performed in 37 dwellings north of Copenhagen, Den-
mark. During a period of 9-12 months, depending on the specific dwelling, measurements of indoor
climate, outdoor climate, window movements, and presence were carried out. The total number
of windows included in the models was 179.
In order to include diversity of occupant behaviour, the models were made separately for each
dwelling. Hence, the behavioural patterns modelled in this thesis belonged to a dwelling and not
an individual occupant. Due to lack of data from some dwellings, the investigation of window
operation behaviour resulted in 35 window opening models, 34 window closing models, and 33
opening angle models for different dwellings.
To get on overview of the process behind this thesis, figure 1 presents the included phases.
Figure 1: Overview of the phases in this thesis
The first phase, background study, included understanding the theory behind occupant behaviour
and the challenges which are linked to modelling occupant behaviour. Furthermore, the back-
ground study covered getting to know the research project as well as understanding and testing
the measuring equipment. The second phase, calibration in dwellings, consisted of visits in the
dwellings where calibrations were performed on the measuring devices which measured the window
movements. The third phase, data handling, consisted of collecting a comprehensive amount of
data, cleaning it, merging it, and preparing it for the fourth phase; deriving models. Finally, the
fifth phase, reflection of measured data and final models, included a presentation, reflection, and
discussion of the results.
1.1
Background
This master thesis was performed in collaboration with the International Centre for Indoor En-
vironment and Energy, ICIEE, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU. The thesis was a
2 of 253
==PAGE:17==
1.1. Background
part of a larger research project called UserTEC which worked in close collaboration with DTU,
among others. UserTEC was a five year long multidisciplinary research project lead by Kirsten
Gram-Hanssen at SBi, Aalborg University, and financed by Innovation Fund Denmark [41], [39].
The aim of the UserTEC project was to investigate everyday life in households and hereby improve
the correlation between the building user and building technology to decrease energy consump-
tion [40]. UserTEC consisted of four working packages where the research project in this thesis
belonged to number three: WP3 develops and tests new user adapted energy efficient building
technologies [39], [41].
Prior to this thesis, three other master students were working on the research project in collabo-
ration with Rune Korsholm Andersen at DTU. The aims of their master thesis’ were as follows:
• Tasnim Azzouz: Modelling of occupant behaviour in regards to presence in dwellings [38].
• Anne Sloth Bidstrup: Investigation of change in occupant behaviour when receiving
feedback on indoor climate parameters [5].
• Maren Lea Kirstein: Further development of the presence model made by Tasnim Azzouz
[27].

2
Theory
This chapter will describe why modelling of occupant behaviour is important in the built sector.
Furthermore, it will introduce theory about underlying reasons for why occupants behave as they
do, and how occupant behaviour can be modelled through different approaches. Theory about
the mathematical and statistical approach used in this thesis to derive the models will also be
presented. Lastly, the indoor and outdoor climate parameters which the models were based on,
among others, will shortly be introduced.
2.1
Occupant behaviour
Occupant behaviour is a term used in the built sector which covers the people occupying a building,
their interactions with the building as well as actions targeted towards themselves. This can be in
terms of opening or closing windows, adjusting blinds or curtains, adjusting thermostats, adjusting
clothing levels, etc.
5 of 253
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 2 | Theory
2.1.1
The reason for modelling occupant behaviour
Energy performance and indoor climate of buildings as well as how these two parameters interact
with each other have for many years been thoroughly investigated and understood. However, the
ability of predicting energy use still becomes more and more inaccurate in an adverse way as the
buildings get more energy efficient [6], [23]. This discrepancy is known as the so-called energy gap;
the gap between predicted and actual energy use of buildings. An example of this is illustrated
in figure 2 where the average predicted energy use of 230,233 Danish residents is compared to the
average actual energy use of 135,443 Danish residents. The line on each column represents the
dispersion around the average +/- the standard deviation [19].
Figure 2: Conceptual illustration of predicted vs. real energy performance of buildings. Adapted from [19]
The energy gap is simply caused by a more random parameter interacting with the buildings, its
energy performance and indoor climate; namely occupants. It is already known that occupant
behaviour has a large effect on energy performance of buildings [37], [35], [49], [2], [34]. Actually,
the energy use of the same building may differ with up to 50% only due to differences in occupant
behaviour [37].
Throughout the past decades, green and energy efficient buildings have been high on the agenda
in the construction industry due to a world wide interest of decreasing energy usage. Even though
green buildings are built to be energy efficient, it might not always be the most suitable solution for
all kinds of occupants [49]. Actually, the more energy efficient a building is, the more the energy
performance will be affected by occupant behaviour [34]. Occupants often interact differently
with building control systems than they are intended to in order to use the building correctly.
There is also large diversity in how occupants interact due to different personal preferences [23].
In addition, a simple fact that occupants have the knowledge of being in a high or low energy
consuming building can lead to unexpected behavioural patterns [34]. This is for example seen in
cases where buildings have gone through a comprehensive renovation where the main purpose was
6 of 253
==PAGE:21==
2.1. Occupant behaviour
saving energy. However, the energy savings after the renovation have not been as high as expected
since people act differently knowing that the building is now energy efficient. This phenomenon
is known as the rebound effect [34], [15]. By involving the occupants in an energy strategy and
educating them in how to use the technology most efficiently, the energy gap will be lowered [37],
[34].
Occupant behaviour does not only influence the energy performance of buildings, it also has a large
impact on the indoor climate [2]. Since humans spend about 90% of their time inside buildings
[49], it is beneficial to be able to predict reliable indoor climate conditions. Building performance
in regards to both energy and indoor climate highly depends on whether control systems for e.g.
heating, window openings, and blinds are available to the occupant [49]. Window openings have
been studied for more than 25 years and is one of the most used actions made by the occupants
to adapt to the local environment, which has a huge impact on energy performance and thermal
comfort [21], [37]. It is conceivable that especially in residential buildings, personal preferences
have a high influence on how the occupants control their indoor climate since this is a private
sphere. Additionally, control of technical systems are more available in private homes compared
to common offices [34].
Due to the importance of peoples actions in buildings, the necessity of both understanding occu-
pant behaviour patterns and appliance of occupant models arises. An implementation of stochastic
occupant behavioural models in simulation programs will provide more realistic predictions of both
energy consumption and indoor climate. Additionally, occupant behaviour models will result in
both highly improved design processes and evaluations of energy consumption [37].
2.1.2
Influential drivers
In order to model occupant behaviour, it is necessary to understand the reasons behind the
occupants’ actions. There are several parameters which can drive occupants towards actions such
as opening of windows, adjusting thermostats, rolling blinds up or down, etc. These parameters are
called drivers. It is important to notice that human behaviour not only is affected by environmental
factors such as e.g.
temperature, but that many different drivers (or a combination between
drivers) can trigger a specific action.
Drivers can be grouped into the different categories as
illustrated in figure 3 [37].
7 of 253
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 2 | Theory
Figure 3: Categorization of drivers. From [37]
A further description of what lies beneath each categorization is given below.
• Environmental factors: Actions affected by indoor and outdoor climate conditions, e.g.
temperature, CO2-concentration, wind speed, rain fall, and relative humidity.
• Time-related factors: Actions affected by certain time periods, e.g.
time of day and
season.
• Contextual factors: Actions affected by the context of the building or room, e.g. country,
dwelling type, room type, orientation, building insulation, ventilation type, and heating
system.
• Physiological factors: Actions affected by physiology of occupant, e.g. age, gender, and
BMI.
• Psychological factors: Actions affected by the psychological state of the occupant, e.g.
perception of thermal comfort.
• Social factors: Actions affected by the social context of the occupant, e.g. level of edu-
cation, habits, smoker/non-smoker, presence of the occupant, single person or family, and
number of people living in the residence.
• Random factors: Actions affected by uncertain and unidentifiable factors.
Since there are so many different underlying reasons and influencing factors behind each action,
8 of 253
==PAGE:23==
2.1. Occupant behaviour
occupant behaviour can be very difficult to predict. The drivers cover many different sciences;
natural sciences, social sciences, and economy, and in order to make predictions more precise, it
therefore requires a multidisciplinary approach including more than one group of drivers. This
can, however, be extremely comprehensive which is why most studies so far focus on one or a few
types of drivers [34], see 3 Preliminary studies.
2.1.3
Modelling approach
Modelling of occupant behaviour can be done through different approaches depending on many
different variables. This section will first of all describe in general how occupant behaviour can
be modelled. Then, a more detailed description of the theory of the mathematical method used
to derive the models in this thesis will be described.
2.1.3.1
Deterministic vs. stochastic
The overall methodology of a model depends on whether it is deterministic or stochastic. The
deterministic modelling approach describes coherent causes for different kinds of events [29]. This
means that a number of known parameters will determine whether an event will take place or not.
When speaking of occupant behaviour in regards to window openings, the following examples
describe deterministic modelling of an action:
• When the temperature reaches a certain level, the occupant will open the window.
• When the time is 6pm, the occupant will open the window (assumed to be done when
cooking dinner).
• When the CO2-concentration reaches a certain level, the occupant will open the window if
and only if the outdoor temperature is not below a specific point (the last part is added to
avoid cooling the room down too much during e.g. winter).
These are all examples of how to include occupant behaviour deterministically. However, this
modelling approach does not provide a realistic view of how an occupant acts since it assumes
exactly how and when the occupant will perform a given action. In reality, the behaviour of an
occupant is characterized by randomness. This is not included in the deterministic modelling
approach.
Contrary to deterministic modelling, the stochastic modelling approach describes events which
occur randomly [29]. The stochastic model takes the randomness into account by not determining
when a certain event will happen but determining the probability of an event happening. The
following example describes how window opening behaviour can be implemented stochastically:
• Based on observations on indoor climate and window openings made for a number of oc-
cupants for a certain amount of time, statistical analysis will be used to determine the
9 of 253
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 2 | Theory
probability of a window opening happening. The action will therefore not be directly caused
by e.g. temperature and CO2-concentration. The probability will depend on a number of
parameters such as temperature, CO2-concentration, time of day, etc.
Thus, the stochastic modelling approach takes more versatile behaviour into account than the
deterministic modelling approach.
In simulation programs, the randomness is included by a
random number between 0-1 being held up against the probability; if the probability is larger
than the random number, the action will happen.
Deterministic modelling of occupant behaviour has already been well developed and widely used
for implementation of predicting behaviour in buildings as conventional schedules in simulation
software [34]. Deterministic models representing occupant behaviour are beneficial when looking
at the extent of the work behind the model; it is a simplification of a complex situation which is
easier and less time consuming to produce and implement in simulation programs. However, the
lack of stochastic actions made by occupants undermine the real situation and cause unreliable
predictions [21]. Even though the stochastic modelling approach requires another level of detailed
and comprehensive study, researchers within the field of occupant behaviour find it highly relevant
for the validity of the model that stochastic behaviour is taken into account [6], [21].
2.1.3.2
Generalized linear model; fixed vs. mixed
In this thesis, a stochastic generalized linear model with logistic regression is used as the mathe-
matical approach when modelling the probability of opening and closing windows. This approach
is chosen since it is relatively simple and still manages to take multiple parameters (CO2, air
temperature, RH, etc.) into account by using multivariate logistic regression. In addition, logistic
regression seems to be the preferred statistical method by other researchers modelling window
opening behaviour [25]. This section will therefore present the theory behind this statistical ap-
proach.
A generalized linear model, GLM, is an extension of what is called a basic linear model. The
basic linear model, LM, is expressed by equation 2.1 and is in this case a univariate model; it only
contains one variable.
y = α + βx
(2.1)
where α is the intercept and β is the slope coefficient.
In order to take more than one explanatory variable into account, the linear model can be expanded
to a multivariate linear model by adding up to n number of independent variables.
This is
illustrated in equation 2.2.
y = α + β0x0 + β1x1 + ... + βnxn
(2.2)
10 of 253
==PAGE:25==
2.1. Occupant behaviour
From the basic linear model, a GLM can be derived by transforming the left-hand side of the
model so that it no longer contains a certain value but a function providing a probability [36].
More precise, a GLM consists of the following three elements [44]:
1. A probability distribution: The outcome of the model is a probability distribution which
belongs to the exponential family. The distribution type can vary between different distri-
butions such as binomial and Bernoulli.
2. A linear predictor: The independent variables in the model are of linear coherence as seen
on the right-hand side of equation 2.1 and 2.2. The β-coefficients in the linear predictor are
fixed values and cannot change in the given model.
3. A link function: The dependent variable, y, has gone through a transformation and is
in a GLM a function which links to linearity. The solution to the link function results in
a probability distribution.
The specific link function varies with the type of probability
distribution where e.g. the binomial and Bernoulli distribution belong to the so-called logit
function.
The GLM is therefore used when the needed output of the model is a probability. Since the
independent variables of a GLM are determined, it is a fixed model. The GLM can, however,
be expanded to a generalized linear mixed model, GLMM, where the independent variables are
given by fixed values in normal distributions. With normal distribution as variable means that
the model chooses a β-coefficient randomly from the determined normal distribution. This gives
a mixture between fixed and random effects [46]; hence a mixed model. The GLM and GLMM
are therefore two versions of a linear model which provide a probability distribution based on two
different methods even though the link functions are identical.
Logistic regression
Logistic regression deals with the probability of a binary response; the input of the model being
either 0 ( = false) or 1 ( = true), and is obtained by applying logit as link function to the
GLM/GLMM. The link function logit is given by
ln
(
p
1 −p
)
(2.3)
where p is the probability.
Thus, logistic regression containing multiple explanatory variables is given by
ln
(
p
1 −p
)
= α + β0x0 + β1x1 + ... + βnxn
(2.4)
11 of 253
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2 | Theory
From equation 2.4, the probability p can be derived to
p =
1
1 + e−(α+β0x0+β1x1+...+βnxn)
(2.5)
By using the log-function ln, the outcome of the model, the probability p, can never be less
than 0 or larger than 1. This is beneficial when modelling the probability of window openings and
closings since the probability then is limited to 0-100%. The linear coherence in this mathematical
approach is seen when the probability p is inserted in the link function; then the result will be
a straight line, a plan, or n-dimensional depending on the number of explanatory variables [18].
It must therefore be assumed that the data behind the model is of linear coherence when using
logistic regression.
Logistic regression as GLM or GLMM
When modelling occupant behaviour it can be argued that both logistic regression as a GLM and
GLMM will be the way to go. As previously mentioned, the diversity of occupant behaviour is
an important aspect to implement in the model. This can be done in different ways by e.g. using
either GLM or GLMM.
Theoretically, the GLMM is the correct statistical method to use since it directly implements the
occupant diversity through the mixed effects; the β-coefficients are chosen randomly from a specific
normal distribution and can therefore belong to any kind of occupant behaviour. Furthermore,
it is possible to make one combined model when using GLMM representing all the investigated
behavioural patterns and still take the diversity into account. However, there are some disadvan-
tages when dealing with GLMMs. Firstly, the methodology is comprehensive and time consuming
compared to GLMs. Secondly, mixed effects have only been on the agenda during the last couple
of decades due to the lack of computer capacity and is, to the knowledge of the authors, not
fully examined yet within the field of occupant behaviour. Thirdly, and most importantly, the
use of a GLMM with multivariate logistic regression can result in under- or over-predicted cases
when implementing the model in simulation software. The following two scenarios will be used to
describe cases like this:
• Scenario 1: Data from one dwelling shows that the window will be opened during summer
time with high outdoor temperatures even though the indoor CO2-concentration is low.
Here the outdoor temperature will be the main predictor.
• Scenario 2: Data from another dwelling shows that the CO2-concentration of a room should
be high with cold outdoor temperatures before the window will be opened. Here the indoor
CO2-concentration will be the main predictor.
These two scenarios explain two different behavioural patterns.
The scenarios have the same
predictors but do not agree on which predictor is the main predictor. When the GLMM randomly
12 of 253
==PAGE:27==
2.1. Occupant behaviour
chooses the coefficients representing these predictors, it might choose both the highest predictor
from scenario 1 and 2 at the same time. This interaction between the scenarios will therefore
cause an unrealistic large probability of a window opening; an over-predicted case. Since the use
of mixed effects is relatively new within this field, there has not yet been found a way out of this
unfortunate situation without a comprehensive amount of work [1].
Contrary to the GLMM, a GLM does not directly take diversity into account. It is therefore
necessary to make several models representing each of the investigated behavioural patterns. Di-
versity is then included by randomly choosing one of the patterns when implementing the model
in simulation software. The GLM has the advantage of being simple and easy to perform and it
has already been widely used for behavioural models. Even though it might seem comprehensive
to make a larger number of models, the results coming from this method will be more reliable.
Based on this, it can be concluded that the GLM will be the preferred method to use in this
thesis.
Significance of explanatory variables
In order to evaluate the significance of the explanatory variables which are included in a model,
a so-called p-value can be used. The smaller the p-value, the more significant the explanatory
variable.
Common within statistics; having a p-value below 0.05 means that the explanatory
variable is statistically significant [1].
2.1.3.3
Model training
During the process of deriving a model, the model often undergoes a so-called training phase.
This refers to actions being performed which make the model as good and simple as possible. The
goal during the training phase is to achieve the most simple model that describes the probability
the best. A step in the training of the model is forward and backward selection of explanatory
variables which will be used on the models in this thesis. The theory behind forward and backward
selection is therefore presented in this section.
Forward and backward selection is a method used to determine which variables have a consistent
effect on the probability function [25].
Hence, it is investigated which explanatory variables
should be included in the model and which variables should be excluded. The point is to achieve
a representative model with as few variables as possible since fewer variables means a simpler
model. Having too many variables can result in an overfitted model. The selection method can
be performed in the following four different ways:
1. Forward selection
2. Backward selection
3. Forward/backward selection
13 of 253
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2 | Theory
4. Backward/forward selection
When using either of these methods, explanatory variables will be either added (forward selected)
or removed (backward selected). Hereby, different models based on the same data are obtained
from forward/backward selection. These models have to be compared to each other in order to
find the best model. This is done by using a statistical criteria and comparing the criteria between
the models. The criteria determines whether to discard a model and which variables should be
included/excluded from the model. There are several different criteria which can be the decisive
for the selection of variables. A widely used criteria within this field is the Akaike Information
Criteria, AIC [2], [49], [6], [25], [35]. AIC is an estimator of the relative quality of a statistical
model [42]. If you e.g. have a collection of models based on the same data, AIC will describe
the quality of each model relative to the other models. If one model out of a collection of models
has the lowest AIC, it means that this specific model has the best fit. However, AIC is a relative
measure. It cannot say how low the AIC criteria should be and can therefore not say anything
about the absolute quality of the model. It can only be used in the purpose of comparison with
other models based on the same data. This means that even though a model has the lowest AIC
out of the collection of models, it can in practice still have a poor fit [42]. The AIC is given by:
AIC = 2k −2ln(L)
(2.6)
where k is the number of variables and L is the maximum value of the likelihood function for the
specific distribution type [42]. A likelihood function is a function which determines the goodness
of the fit [45]. As it is seen in equation 2.6, AIC gives a penalty when a new variable is added to
the model. This decreases the possibility of overfitting the model since adding another variable
to a model will always increase the goodness of the fit.
There is a discussion in how much lower the AIC should be between models before discarding
a model. A rule of thumb is that if the difference is more than the logarithm to the degrees of
freedom (number of observations) minus one, log(df-1), then the model with the highest AIC can
be discarded [1].
Other criteria which can be used in the selection of variables are e.g.
R2 and the Bayesian
Information Criteron (BIC). BIC is very closely related to AIC. It is also based on a likelihood
function and also gives penalties for adding extra variables. The only difference in practice is that
the penalty is larger for BIC than for AIC [43]. R2 is an absolute measure between 0 and 1, and
measures the variance explained. However, it is not always appropriate to use R2 as criteria since
it does not punish for extra variables. The goodness of the fit, and hence R2, will always increase
when adding extra variables. Using R2 as a criteria for selection of variables will therefore lead to
overfitted and unnecessarily complex models.
When the researcher has chosen which criteria will be the basis of the selection of variables, the
14 of 253
==PAGE:29==
2.1. Occupant behaviour
selection process can be performed. In the following, the four types of selection processes will be
explained on the basis of using AIC as selection criteria.
Forward selection is a process where the logistic regression model is formed from the bottom and
upwards; beginning with one variable and ending with n number of variables. An extra variable
is added one at a time. The steps are as follows:
1. Univariate logistic regression: Firstly, a univariate logistic regression is derived for
each explanatory variable. If there e.g. are three explanatory variables, there will be three
univariate regressions. These will be compared to a model with a constant distribution where
the right side of equation 2.4 is equal to 1. When comparing the models, the regression with
the lowest AIC is picked as the winner model if and only if the difference between the AICs
is larger than log(df-1). If the difference is smaller, the model with the larger AIC will be
chosen. This is conceptually illustrated in figure 4 (in this step, model 1 represents the
model with a constant distribution).
Figure 4: Example of selection of variables with AIC as selection criteria
2. Bivariate logistic regression: The winner model from step 1 is used as a basis to make
bivariate logistic regressions. A bivariate regression is made for each remaining variable.
Continuing the example from step 1, there will now be two bivariate models to be compared
with the winner model from step 1. Of this there are two outcomes:
(a) The new winner model is bivariate: The model continues the forward selection process
in step 3.
(b) The new winner model is univariate: The selection process stops and the model de-
scribing the probability the best has thus been found in step 1.
3. Multivariate logistic regression: If the bivariate model is selected from step 2a, the
forward selection continues. Stepwise, new variables are added to the model, and by contin-
uously checking AIC as in figure 4, the explanatory variables are either included or discarded.
15 of 253
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 2 | Theory
The backward selection process is the opposite of forward selection; the logistic regression model is
derived from the top and downwards beginning with the maximum number of variables and ending
with n numbers of variables. One variable at a time is removed from the model by continuously
comparing with the criteria. Forward/backward and backward/forward selection is bidirectional
and is a combination between the previously explained methods. At each step the variables are
tested for inclusion or exclusion of the model.
2.1.3.4
Checking the fit of the models
When a model is derived it is preferable to check whether the fit is good or poor. If a model is based
on a multivariate regression of more than three dimensions, it is not possible to plot it. Therefore,
it is necessary to use another way than looking at the data points versus the plotted model to
evaluate whether the fit is good or poor. For a linear model (including GLM and GLMM), the
following six points are assumed [48]:
1. Linearity
2. Homoscedasticity
3. Normality of residuals
4. Absence of collinearity
5. No influential data points
6. Independence
The liability of these assumptions are evaluated in order to figure out if the fit is good or poor.
The assumptions of points 1-3 can be checked by looking at residual plots of the model. Residuals
are the distance from the data points to the model line/plan/n-dimensional figure. In an example
of a linear model, these are illustrated as red lines in figure 5.
Figure 5: Residuals. From [48]
16 of 253
==PAGE:31==
2.1. Occupant behaviour
A residual plot is formed by ”slicing” out the residuals and rotating the plot, see figure 6 and 7.
In figure 7, the x-axis is fitted values for LMs and predicted values for GLMs/GLMMs.
Figure 6: Residual ”slicing”. From [48]
Figure 7: Residuals vs. fitted values. From [48]
Linearity
The assumption of linearity can be checked on a residual plot as figure 7. In the case illustrated
in figure 7, there is no specific pattern between the residuals. However, if the residuals form a
curve or some other nonlinear pattern, it indicates that the assumption of linearity is violated.
An example of this is shown in figure 8 [48].
Figure 8: Residual plot showing violation of linearity assumption. From [48]
The goal is thus to retrieve a residual plot with no specific pattern. An example of a good residual
plot for confirming linearity is shown in figure 9.
17 of 253
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 2 | Theory
Figure 9: Residual plot confirming linearity and homoscedasticity assumption. From [48]
If the plot shows that there is a nonlinear correlation, there are several things which can be
done; 1) adding a missing variable that has an important effect on the linearity, 2) transformation
of variables, e.g. squaring the value of a specific variable, or 3) transformation of the response
[48].
Homoscedasticity assumption
The assumption of homoscedasticity is also checked on the residual plot. Homoscedasticity means
that the residuals are homogeneously scattered in the plot. This is the case in figure 9. This
means that the variances in the model are equally distributed around the zero line in the residual
plot. If the homoscedasticity assumption is violated, so-called heteroscedasticity is present in the
model; meaning that there are unequal variances. An example of heteroscetdasticity is shown in
figure 10 [48].
Figure 10: Residual plot with heteroscedasticity. From [48]
18 of 253
==PAGE:33==
2.1. Occupant behaviour
It is seen that the residuals are smaller for lower fitted values than for larger fitted values. This
means that the model better describes the small values than the large values which is problematic.
The conclusion of the plot in figure 10 is that the model is not homoscedastic [48].
Normality of residuals
The assumption of normality of residuals is the least important of the six assumptions [48]. Lin-
ear models are quite robust towards violation of this assumption. If the residuals are normally
distributed, the assumption is confirmed. This can be checked by looking at a histogram of the
residuals which preferably should be bell-shaped [48]. Furthermore, the normality of residuals
can be checked by a so-called normal Q-Q plot where the data points preferably should be on a
straight line; also confirming a normal distribution of the residuals. An example of a histogram
and normal Q-Q plot which confirms the assumption of normality of residuals is shown in figure
11.
Figure 11: Histogram of residuals (left) and normal Q-Q plot (right) confirming normality of residuals.
From [48]
If the assumption of normality cannot be confirmed, the normal distributions of the data behind
the explanatory variables should be checked. When the explanatory variables consist of data with
symmetric normal distributions, the model residuals are more likely to have a bell-shaped normal
distribution [1]. If there is not found a symmetric normal distribution behind an explanatory
variable, this variable can be transformed so that the distribution gets more symmetric. By this
means that any chosen function can be applied to the explanatory variable if it improves the goal
of obtaining a symmetric normal distribution. Such action should, however, be taken into account
when evaluating the results.
Absence of collinearity
If two explanatory variables are related to each other, they are said to be collinear [48]. A good
example of this is relative humidity and temperature. Relative humidity is related to temperature
and, therefore, these two variables will be collinear. If there are collinear variables in a model
it can become unstable and the influence of a collinear variable can be wiped away by the other
variable [48]. This is of course a challenge when deriving the occupant behaviour models.
19 of 253
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 2 | Theory
Influential data points
If there are influential data points it can lead to an unstable model [48]. An influential data point
is a point which can change the model drastically if it is excluded. If the slope of the model
e.g. doubles when a specific data point is excluded, it is a sign that the data point is extremely
influential. If influential data points are found, the data point cannot simply be removed; however,
the model could be calculated with and without this data point and afterwards compare the results.
The only time it is okay to remove data points is if there, without a doubt, is an error in the data
point [48]. This could e.g. be if there is a temperature measurement of 200◦C. Then, there is no
doubt that the data point is an incorrect measuring point.
Independence
The assumption of independence is the most important of all assumptions [48]. Independence
is when data points are independent of each other. An example of independent data is flipping
a coin; each time the coin is flipped the outcome has nothing to do with the previous outcome
meaning that the data is independent. When combining data from several subjects into one model
each subject should only be represented once in the dataset to eliminate the risk of dependent
data. However, when making a model for a single subject, several measurements on the same
subject are allowed [1]. This is the case in this thesis where a unique model is made for each
dwelling.
If the mentioned six assumptions can be confirmed, it is a sign that the derived linear model is a
good fit to the data.
2.1.3.5
Validation of models
An important process of creating a model representing reality is the validation of the model and
its outcome. The usage of a model highly depends on the representativeness of the model; it will
never generate the truth itself but it can be capable of providing a good representation of a real
situation. Validations can be done in several ways; e.g. testing the model in simulation software
or with an external validation approach.
External validation uses data from other studies to validate the potential of the model. After the
model completion, external cross validation can be applied as a blind methodology where a model
developed on dataset A is used to simulate dataset B and vice versa [35]. Before evaluating the
potential of the model on external data, it is important to check how well the model reproduces
its own data. If the model is not representative to its own data, application of the model on other
datasets will be unreliable.
A models validity can also be checked by implementing it in a simulation software. The results of
the simulation can then be checked and evaluated if they are realistic or not.
20 of 253
==PAGE:35==
2.2. Indoor climate
2.2
Indoor climate
Since the models in this thesis were based on indoor climate parameters, among others, a short
theory behind these will be presented in this section. The indoor environment has a huge effect
on how humans behave inside buildings since indoor climate parameters influence how the climate
is perceived by the occupants. The perception of the climate leads towards actions for controlling
the indoor environment by e.g. opening the windows.
When speaking of indoor climate, the following parameters are of relevance:
• Thermal comfort: Air temperature, draught, relative humidity, mean radiant temperature
• Atmospheric comfort: Volatile organic compounds (VOC), CO2-concentration, odour,
relative humidity, etc.
• Optic comfort: Artificial light, daylight
• Acoustic comfort: Sound pressure level, reverberation time
The models in this thesis did not take optic and acoustic parameters into account. Within thermal
and atmospheric comfort the only included variables in the models were air temperature, relative
humidity, and CO2-concentration. Therefore, just these variables will be described.
2.2.1
CO2
The indoor air quality, IAQ, is affected by many different parameters and emission sources. When
looking at residential buildings, IAQ depends on e.g. the number of occupants, kinds of activ-
ities such as cooking and smoking, emissions from furniture and building materials, duration of
occupancy and activities, etc. [14].
IAQ can be investigated by either measuring the level of volatile organic compounds (VOC) or
CO2. In this master thesis, CO2 is used as an indicator of IAQ since it is easy to measure and
also can be used as a presence indicator. According to DS/EN 15251 [14], the levels given in
table 1 define the acceptable ranges of CO2 in dwellings.
The outdoor CO2-concentration is
approximately 400 ppm in Denmark.
Table 1: Recommended CO2-concentration above outdoor level [14]
Category
CO2-concentration above outdoor [ppm]
I
350
II
500
III
800
IV
<800
When the CO2-concentration reaches a certain level, the IAQ will be unacceptable. In order to
21 of 253
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 2 | Theory
lower the concentration of CO2, a change of air is required. This can either be done through
mechanical or natural ventilation.
2.2.2
Air temperature
Thermal comfort describes the feeling of being satisfied with the thermal environment [24]. Ther-
mal comfort is perceived differently due to difference in preferences caused by both physiological
and psychological factors. It is therefore impossible to specify an environment that will provide
thermal satisfaction for everyone [24]. Danish Standards have made design recommendations of
how the operative air temperature should be depending on the activity level (MET), clothing
level (clo), and time of year. The recommendations are based on predicted satisfaction of which
a certain percentage of occupants will find the temperature acceptable. According to DS/EN
15251 [14], living spaces such as bedroom, kitchen, and living room in residential buildings with
an assumed activity level of 1.2met have the design operative air temperatures as presented in
table 2.
Table 2: Recommended operative temperature for residential buildings [14]
Category
-
Min. operative temperature,
winter, 1 clo [◦C]
Max. operative temperature,
summer, 0.5 clo [◦C]
I

25.5
II
20
26
III
18
27
As seen in table 2, there are different recommended temperatures depending on the given season
of the year. In order to adjust the thermal environment, cooling or heating of the air will be
needed. Some of the main actions for controlling the indoor air temperature in naturally ventilated
buildings are opening windows and changing thermostat settings.
2.2.3
Relative humidity
The relative humidity, RH, describes the amount of water in the air and is an important indoor
climate parameter when speaking of both health effects from buildings and building damages such
as condensations causing mold. RH is given by:
RH =
actual amount of water vapor in the air
maximum amount of water vapor in the air · 100%
(2.7)
The acceptable levels of relative humidity inside dwellings according to DS/EN 15251 [14] are
given in table 3.

As seen in table 3, the air can be either too humid or too dry and needs to have a relative humidity

2.3
Outdoor climate
Occupants’ interaction with the building, including window operation, is highly affected by out-
door climatic conditions [17]. It has therefore been important also to include variables describing
outdoor climatic conditions when predicting window operation in this thesis. The variables in-
cluded in this thesis are outdoor temperature, outdoor RH, rain, wind angle and strength, and
gust angle and strength. However, there are also other variables which can be of influence; solar
radiation, snowfall, outdoor air pollution, noise level, etc.
Outdoor temperature is one of the most influential outdoor climatic conditions in regards to
window operation [17]. Many investigations have shown that there is a direct linear correlation
between outdoor temperature and window use in the range of outdoor temperatures between
−10◦C and +25◦C [17]. Wind speed is also a significant indicator of occupants’ window operation,
as higher wind speeds lead to decreasing probabilities of window openings [17].

==PAGE:39==
3
Preliminary studies
This chapter will describe some of the studies which so far have been made within the field of
occupant behaviour, the findings and limitations of the research studies, and the validity of the
created models.
3.1
Aim of research studies
A general aim for most research studies was to determine which drivers were the most influential
in regards to a certain type of occupant behaviour; opening/closing of windows, opening/closing
of blinds, adjusting thermostats, adjusting clothing level, etc. Most studies focused on how these
actions were influenced by environmental drivers as well as time-related drivers but did not im-
plement contextual and social drivers due to comprehensiveness of such studies [34].
Samuelson et al. [33] studied how the indoor and outdoor temperature, time of day, and CO2-
concentration could be used to determine the probability of occupants opening or closing a window
in a dormitory. Cali et al. [6] investigated how the use of windows in German residential buildings
were affected by indoor and outdoor environmental conditions, and Andersen et al. [2] made
a similar study in 15 Danish dwellings. A study by Haldi and Robinson [21] tried to predict
25 of 253
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 3 | Preliminary studies
occupants personal actions (clothing level, activity level, drinking) as well as environmental actions
(adjusting windows, doors, fans, and blinds). These are only a few of the studies made within the
field of occupant behaviour.
In regards to investigation of window opening angles, which was a focus area in this thesis, not
many investigations have been made yet, especially not when it comes to residential buildings. In
Andersen et al.’s study of 15 Danish dwellings [2], only 3 rooms were equipped with measuring
equipment able to measure the opening angle of the windows, and the paper did not mention the
outcome of the study of angles. In Switzerland, a study was made of two residential buildings
where opening angles also were measured [35]. In 1990, Fritsch investigated the window opening
angle in offices and grouped the opening angles into six states; five states of opening angles and one
state representing a closed window [6]. Hence, to the knowledge of the authors of this thesis, no
comprehensive study taking the exact opening angles into account in dwellings has been published
yet.
3.2
Modelling methods
During the past years, several different methods have been used to model occupant behaviour.
There has not yet been established or determined a generalized method for this type of modelling
[34]. However, an agreement between each study was that deterministic modelling was not ad-
equate enough to predict occupant behaviour and, therefore, many studies developed stochastic
models [33], [35], [20], [49], [23], [6], [21], [2], [25].
Even though the aim of the studies were similar to each other, the modelling methods differed.
An issue which was discussed in the studies regarding window openings was whether the window
state was modelled, i.e. open or closed, or the change in window state was modelled, i.e. from
closed to open and vice verse. According to Andersen et al. [2], a general trend was to model
the window state rather than the change in state which lead to problematic results. The reason
is that the predictive variable(s) (e.g. indoor temperature) is affected by the window state it is
trying to predict. If a window e.g. is opened on a cool day the indoor temperature will decrease
while the window is open and hereby result in an illogical probability occurring; decreasing indoor
temperatures will then lead to a higher probability of window opening. Andersen et al. [2] therefore
suggested modelling the change in state rather than the state itself since it would eliminate this
issue.
Many previous studies modelled the occupant behaviour in a generalized matter using average
behavioural models without taking diversity into account. According to Haldi et al. [20], this
could be a major problem when trying to predict the energy use and indoor climate conditions of
a building. Some studies, on the other hand, made categorizations of occupants; e.g. passive versus
active occupants, and in that way took some sort of difference between occupants into account
26 of 253
==PAGE:41==
3.3. Findings
[37]. Haldi et al. [23] pointed out the importance of integrating the diversity of occupants in
models since people have individual patterns and act differently due to differences in age, gender,
habits, perception of indoor climate, etc. Therefore, Haldi et al. suggested to use mixed effect
models.
Between the research made so far, there was also a difference in which mathematical and statistical
method was used to create the occupant behavioural model. Among the most popular methods
used were Markov chains and multivariate logistic regressions. Haldi [20] e.g. used GLMM with
multivariate logistic regression for predicting the occupants’ actions on windows, blinds, and
electrical lighting in an office building in Lausanne, Switzerland. Andersen et al. [2] and Cali
et al. [6] both used GLM with multivariate logistic regression for determining the probability of
an occupant action in respectively Danish and German dwellings. When speaking in a general
matter, no statistical approach was identified to be better than others and so far the choice of
mathematical modelling method is up to the researcher.
Common for many studies, the used model training method was forward and backward selection.
The goodness of the model was checked mostly by AIC but also by BIC or R2 during the selection
process [2], [6], [25], [21]. Furthermore, a so-called k-fold cross validation was used by Cali et
al. [6], Zhao et al. [49], and Schweiker et al. [35] which is an internal validation method where
a model is created on subsets of the data and then evaluated based on an additional subset of
data from the same study. The method was used to find the most suitable model for the given
study. However, when speaking of a mixed effects model, e.g. the one previously mentioned by
Haldi et al. [23], a forward/backward selection process has not yet been possible due to very
comprehensive and large datasets [1]. In respect to the selection process of variables there was,
again, no standardized approach yet.
3.3
Findings
In many studies, indoor and outdoor temperatures were evaluated as the most important drivers
for window openings and closings [37].
Andersen et al.
[2], however, argued that the indoor
air quality had an effect on the occupant’s tendency to open windows.
Andersen et al.
also
argued that the driver for opening a window might be different from the driver for closing a
window.
Therefore, differentiating between these two actions is important.
Other influential
drivers affecting the probability of window actions found in some studies were rain fall, wind
speed, indoor relative humidity, and global solar radiation [6], [2], [25].
Furthermore, many researchers agreed that the time of day and season was an important factor
influencing occupants opening and closing behaviour [6], [2],[25], [37]. In residential buildings, the
probability of window openings was higher in the mornings and evenings due to e.g. showering and
cooking [37]. In many studies, it was also determined that there were differences in behavioural
27 of 253
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 3 | Preliminary studies
tendencies respective of the season [35], [2], [37]. In the two residential buildings in Switzerland
[35], large opening angles were more likely to occur during winter than summer. Their explanation
for this was that in the winter time, occupants opened the windows less frequently but with a
large angle, and in the summer the windows were opened for longer time but with smaller angles
[35].
In a study on an office building, Haldi and Robinson [22] found that occupants tended to react on
the indoor environment when moving from one place to another. The probability of interacting
with windows was highest during the time right after arriving as well as right before departing
[22].
Researchers agreed on the fact that other drivers could be equally influential but were often not
investigated in their studies; such as contextual, social, physiological, and psychological driven
factors [37].
Haldi [23] found that occupant diversity had an extremely large impact on energy demand of
buildings and therefore underlined the importance of implementing diversity in behavioural mod-
els. He found that modelling mixed effects had standard deviations up to three to four times
the magnitude of fixed effect models, which emphasized the importance of occupant diversity
[23].
Furthermore, it was a common finding between all studies that modelling occupant behaviour
stochastically rather than deterministically was preferable when wanting to predict the energy
demand and indoor climate conditions of a building [34], [35], [20], [23], [21], [28], [49], [37], [6],
[2], [25], [33].
3.4
Validation of models
When looking at previous occupant behaviour models, the validity was debatable and there was
no guideline for how to check the model. Actually, most of the existing behavioural models were
only validated throughout the training phase of the model itself in order to find the most suitable
model for the given study.
Schweiker et al. [35] performed a thorough validation of models created on window openings in
residential buildings in Japan and Switzerland. Firstly, the models were checked by using internal
validation where the model reference data was used to reproduce the real situation. It appeared
that the Swiss model was capable of reproducing the window states within an acceptable range.
Secondly, an external cross validation was applied after the model completion where the models
created on residential buildings in Japan and Switzerland were examined by using the dataset
from the other country as validation data [35]. Contrary to the internal validation of the Swiss
28 of 253
==PAGE:43==
3.5. Limitations
model, it was not possible to reproduce the Swiss window state from the Japanese reference data.
Thirdly, Schweiker et al. [35] made another external cross validation of the Swiss model based on
residential buildings by using data from a Swiss office building as validation data. This validation
showed that it was possible to reproduce the window states for the Swiss office building.
It
therefore appeared that a model created for one type of building might be robust enough and
applicable to another building type as long as the climates were comparable [35].
These validation processes show the importance of validation on external data if the model is
intended to be widely used. Agreeing with that, Haldi stated that improvements of the model
validations were still needed so that a model could be used to predict behaviour in other buildings
than the model reference building [20].
Haldi also pointed out the lack of clear and general
guidelines for these validations [20].
3.5
Limitations
When speaking of the validity of models, an important aspect is to be aware of the limitations
made by the researchers throughout the modelling phase. In previous research studies, different
limitations were made since there was no clear guideline of how to measure and model occupant
behaviour.
All examined previous studies in this literature review modelled occupant behaviour as stochastic
models. Hereby, the studies included the randomness which comes with human behaviour. Hence,
they avoided one of the most influential limitations; exclusion of stochastic behaviour.
Andersen et al.
[2], Haldi [20], and Schweiker et al.
[35] included occupant presence in the
occupant behavioural models meaning that the predictions of behaviour only were done during
presence in the buildings. The lack of occupancy as a part of the behavioural models in other
research studies, e.g. the ones made by Cali et al. [6] and Jones et al. [25], was a limitation which
could lead to false predictions and thereby lead to undermined simulations [23]. Additionally, most
of the models representing occupant behaviour derived one generalized linear fixed model, GLM,
including all the dwellings/offices in the investigation. However, these models did not include the
importance of occupant diversity since they included all dwellings/offices in one GLM. Neither
did they solve the diversity issue by deriving GLMMs. This is a limitation of high importance
since the diversity was found to have a large influence on both the model outcome and the energy
flows through building envelopes [20].
Limitations such as size of participating buildings, number of investigated windows in a building,
choice of measurement parameters, and experimental setup also have a large influence on the
model outcome. The studies made on window operation in dwellings were until now quite limited
as they investigated few dwellings and only covered parts of the dwellings. Jones et al. [25] e.g.
29 of 253
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 3 | Preliminary studies
measured occupants’ window operation in just 10 bedrooms, and Andersen et al. [2] measured
window operation only in the living room and main bedroom in 15 dwellings. Therefore, using
the derived models from such studies for predicting window operation on other buildings than the
investigated can be difficult.
Lastly, the climate in which measurements are made has a large influence on how the occupants
behave which limits the usage of the model for predictions in other climates [35].
30 of 253
==PAGE:45==
4
Modelling strategy
This chapter sums up the theory and preliminary studies and briefly presents which elements from
the theory and preliminary studies were included in the modelling of occupant behaviour in this
master thesis. A more detailed description will be given in 5 Method. The modelling strategy
was based on reflections from the theory and preliminary studies. Some choices were made based
on the experience from previous researchers while others choices were made as attempts to fill in
some of the gaps which were missing in the previous studies. The modelling strategy was formed
through an iterative process continuously throughout the thesis period.
The issues which were reflected upon and which led to the final modelling strategy were:
• Influential drivers: In the models made in this master thesis, several categories of drivers
were taken into account; environmental factors, time-related factors, and contextual factors.
Similar to many of the preliminary studies, other categorizations were not included due to
the comprehensiveness of such studies.
• Deterministic vs. stochastic modelling: In order to include the randomness related
to occupant behaviour, the models were made as stochastic models. It was evaluated by
the authors of the thesis, that this method would lead to the best estimations of how the
windows were operated in the dwellings.
• Modelling window openings and closings: The window opening and window closing
31 of 253
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 4 | Modelling strategy
models were made separalety as suggested by Andersen et al. due to different drivers for
the two actions.
• Occupant diversity: Due to the importance of including diversity as explained by Haldi
[23], this was taken into account. This was, however, not done by using GLMM as Haldi
suggested [23] but by making a separate model per dwelling.
If the models were to be
implemented in a simulation software, the software would randomly choose one of the derived
models. Each simulation would then have different results and hereby take occupant diversity
into account.
• Model training: The models underwent a training phase by using forward and backward
selection with AIC as criteria. This was the chosen method due to its simplicity in compar-
ison with other model training methods. The choice of AIC as selection criteria was based
on the experience from the previous research.
• Window state: Andersen et al.’s advice [2] to model the change in window state rather
than modelling the state itself was used in this thesis. This was chosen in order to reduce
the risk of illogical predictions.
• Occupant presence: A limitation in other studies was not to integrate presence of occu-
pants. In this thesis, occupant presence was included. This was done so the probability of a
window opening or closing only was calculated if an occupant was present in the dwelling.
• Occupant presence status: Known to the authors of this thesis, it has not yet been
investigated on dwellings whether the probability of a window opening was larger during
arrival (as for offices [22]). Therefore, since it was interesting to see whether the same pattern
applied for residential buildings, presence status (e.g. arriving, departing) was included in
this study.
• Number of windows and rooms: The number of windows included in the study in this
thesis was higher than many of the previous studies. 179 windows were in total included
in the study and were distributed in different rooms; bathrooms, livingrooms, kitchens,
bedrooms, etc. Hence, the room type is not limited in this study.
• Opening angles The opening angles were also a part of this study and were included
without categorization like Fritsch [6].
32 of 253
==PAGE:47==
5
Method
This chapter will describe the setup of the research project, explain what type of measuring
equipment was used, how it was tested, calibrated, and how it functioned.
Furthermore, the
method of how the data was collected, filtered, and prepared will be presented.
Finally, the
deriving of the models will thoroughly be gone over.
5.1
Experimental setup
This section describes the basis of the research project which this master thesis is a part of. The
area as well as dwelling, occupant, and window types will be described here.
The research project made experiments in dwellings in a living compound north of Copenhagen
built in 1981[38]. Due to privacy of the participants, the specific location of the housing area
and the participating dwellings were anonymous. The setup of the measuring equipment was
conducted by supervisor Rune Korsholm Andersen and the previous master students Tasnim
Azzouz and Anne Sloth Bidstrup from November 2016 until the beginning of March 2017 [38],[5].
The number of dwellings involved in the project changed over time since some participants wished
to be signed off the program. In March, the number of participating dwellings was 46, and in the
33 of 253
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 5 | Method
time frame of this master thesis, the number of participating dwellings was 37.
A plan view of the housing area is illustrated in figure 12.
Figure 12: Overview of housing area. From [38]
The participating dwellings were all equipped with several measuring devices which measured
occupant actions and indoor climate conditions, see 5.2 Measuring equipment. The dwellings in
the living compound were all rented townhouses. They were clustered in a green area without
access for cars which meant that the noise level in the area was quite low. The dwellings differed in
size and type and were therefore subject to different contextual basis’. The participating dwellings
differed between being one or two stories and were between 65-106.5m2. For plan views of the
dwelling types, see Appendix A. Furthermore, the dwellings were heated through district heat.
The heating system consisted of radiators in all rooms except the bathrooms, which had floor
heating. In regards to ventilation, there was mechanical exhaust in the kitchen and the bathroom.
Fresh air supply solely came from natural ventilation and infiltration. The buildings were poorly
insulated, and many of the occupants explained that they had large heating bills. Furthermore,
many occupants expressed unsatisfying conditions in regards to moisture and damp. Many had
observed large amounts of condensation on the windows, and some had also observed blotches on
the ceiling and/or walls.
In regards to the people living in the area, different types of occupants occurred; families with
children, retired couples, single persons, etc. However, specific facts about the occupants as well
34 of 253
==PAGE:49==
5.2. Measuring equipment
as the number of occupants were not known in this study. In order to get this information, surveys
were necessary which was not the focus in this master thesis, see 7 Discussion.
The windows in the dwellings differed in size from small windows of approximately 30x30cm to
large windows of approximately 150x90cm. There were also glass terrace doors of approximately
200x90cm. The windows had different hinging types; top hinged, bottom hinged, side hinged,
horizontally center hinged, and vertically center hinged. The different hinging types of course
lead to different possible opening angles. Theoretically, the maximum possible opening angles
differed between 45-180◦depending on the window type. However, some occupants had large
plants or other obstacles in front of the windows so that the maximum possible opening degree
was reduced.
5.2
Measuring equipment
This section presents the equipment used to measure in- and outdoor climate parameters, window
operations, and presence in the dwellings.
Throughout the entire research project, the following sensors were used for measurements:
• Wireless sensor tags
– Compass tag: Magnetic compass sensor used to detect window openings, closings,
and opening angles
– Accelerometer tag: Sensor measuring acceleration used to detect window openings,
closings, and opening angles
– PIR KumoSensor: Infrared motion sensor used to detect movements inside the
dwellings
– Reed KumoSensor: Magnetic sensor used to detect whether the doors at main en-
trances were opened or closed
• Netatmo
– Modules: Weather stations used to detect indoor climate parameters in each dwelling
and outdoor climate in the housing area. The modules measured temperature, relative
humidity, CO2-concentration, sound level, and air pressure.
– Wind gauge: Gauge measuring angle and speed of wind and gust
– Rain gauge: Gauge measuring rainfall
35 of 253
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 5 | Method

The compass tags, accelerometer tags, PIR and Reed KumoSensors were all connected to an
ethernet tag manager as shown in figure 13. The ethernet tag manager was connected to the
Wi-Fi-router in the respective dwellings. Data was collected online to a URL-database created
by Associate Professor Jørn Toftum from ICIEE, DTU. The URL was regularly emptied and the
data was saved as Access databases.

sors, see Appendix B.

5.2.1.2
Compass tags
The compass tags measuring window operations are presented in figure 17. The compass tags
measured window movements based on x-, y-, and z-coordinates. Additionally, the compass tag
was equipped with an internal memory so that it could log during offline periods [7].
Figure 17: Compass tag. From [7]
The compass tags were placed on the window glass in the corner of the chosen windows in the
37 of 253
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 5 | Method
dwellings. The number of windows with a compass tag differed from dwelling to dwelling. In
total, 179 windows were monitored with compass tags during the time frame of this master thesis.
The choice of which windows were included in the models was made during the setup based on 1)
conversation with occupants in the respective dwellings who revealed which windows were most
often used and 2) covering as many rooms as possible. Here, it should be kept in mind that the
accelerometer tags also were included when trying to cover all rooms. The tags were placed in all
types of rooms; bedrooms, kitchens, bathrooms, hallways, entrances, living rooms, etc.
Testing compass tag robustness and reliability
To understand the compass tags and how they were influenced by settings and external actions,
they were tested in regards to their robustness of the following; other nearby magnetic fields,
battery level and voltage, and sensitivity settings.
• Influence of other nearby magnetic fields: The coordinates given by the compass tags
were estimated through the surrounding magnetic field. It was therefore tested whether the
compass tag was affected by contact with other magnets. It showed that the coordinates
changed remarkably after interaction with another magnetic field. However, when the ad-
ditional magnet was removed, the magnetic field slowly changed back to the previous field.
In the tests run in this study, the change of fields took approximately one hour. Therefore,
it was of high importance for the validity of the results to keep the compass sensors distinct
from other magnets. It was e.g. important to keep a distance between the compass tags and
the Reed KumoSensors consisting of two magnets which both were installed in the dwellings.
• Influence of change of battery and battery voltage: It was investigated whether the
coordinates were affected by change in the compass tag’s battery capacity. When comparing
the same tag with a battery capacity of 100% and 80%, respectively, it was seen that the
coordinates changed. The span of the coordinate sets were the same but a displacement of the
coordinate set occurred. However, the effect of change in battery level differed drastically
from tag to tag, and no overall coherence between battery level and angles was found.
Therefore, the jumps in coordinates were taken into account when evaluating the data from
the compass tags. Furthermore, it was known from the manufacturer that the battery level
dropped quickly in the first hours and then stabilized and slowly dropped throughout the
battery life time. The battery voltage was also affected by strong temperature changes [8].
• Sensitivity: For each compass tag, it was possible to adjust the sensitivity level which
controlled how sensitive the tag was towards movements. If the sensitivity level was too low
the tag was not able to respond to small movements, e.g. if a window was opened on clamp.
When setting up the measuring equipment, Anne Sloth Bidstrup [5], Tasnim Azzouz [38],
and Rune Korsholm Andersen [1] set all compass tags to have a sensitivity level of 85%.
During the testing of compass tags in this thesis, several issues regarding the sensitivity
settings were found.
38 of 253
==PAGE:53==
5.2. Measuring equipment
First of all, the compass tags automatically downregulated their sensitivity to 50% if the
battery level was too low. This was problematic since it led to window operations not being
registered. It resulted in loss of data until replacement of the battery occurred.
Secondly, the proper sensitivity level differed from tag to tag. Therefore, investigations of
the sensitivity was made and adjusted individually for each tag during a calibration. If a
certain sensitivity level allowed the tag to detect a movement on crack openings (approx.
10◦), this sensitivity level was chosen for the respective tag. The proper sensitivity setting
per tag differed widely and ranged from 65%-98% across all tags.
Lastly, having a too high sensitivity led to misleading measurements. If a window e.g. was
opened at a large angle, it was possible that the compass tag detected the movement under
the way hereby giving a false indication of the opening angle. Furthermore, there could
be several registrations for the same movement.
If a window e.g.
stood closed, several
detections could occur since the tag responded to vibrations if the sensitivity was too high.
The choice of sensitivity settings was therefore a dilemma between:
1. Medium sensitivity: Achieving precise results at large opening angles but not being
able to capture small opening angles.
2. High sensitivity: Achieving imprecise results at large opening angles, receiving more
movement detections than necessary but capturing small opening angles.
By asking the occupants in the dwellings, it was found that many occupants opened their
windows on the clamp or on very small angles. Therefore, it was chosen by the researchers
in collaboration with the supervisor to continue with the second option; high sensitivity.
However, the number of responses for each tag was checked for an unrealistic large amount
of movements after the sensitivity adjustment in order to avoid misleading results.
Calibration of compass tags
It was found by the previous master students, that each tag delivered a unique set of (x,y,z)-
coordinates in which there was no coherence between. Therefore, it was necessary to calibrate
each tag individually in order to determine the opening angle for the specific window.
The
following will describe how the calibration was performed and how the angles were extracted from
the coordinates.
The calibration method is described based on an example tag which was mounted on a door in
the author’s, Nathalie Francisca Henriksen, home. The calibration was performed by moving the
door systematically from opened to closed in the same angle a number of times. The opening
angles were 90◦, 60◦, 45◦, 30◦, 15◦, and 10◦. The readings for each coordinate (x,y,z) were plotted
separately with the number of movements on the horizontal axis and the respective coordinate
reading on the vertical axis; see figure 18 for x-coordinate, figure 19 for y-coordinate, and figure
20 for z-coordinate.
39 of 253
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 5 | Method
Figure 18: X-readings from test compass tag
Figure 19: Y-readings from test compass tag
Figure 20: Z-readings from test compass tag
Figure 18, 19, and 20 give an indication of how the compass tag converted its position into
coordinates. Since the x-coordinates were within the same and rather small interval during the
entire calibration, the x-axis of this tag was identified to be the vertical axis. The y- and z-axis
were therefore the horizontal axis’. The y-coordinates only showed a clear distinction between
90◦and 60◦.
On the other hand, the z-coordinates showed a clear trend for each angle and
were therefore considered the most reliable coordinates for determining the opening angles of this
specific tag.
40 of 253
==PAGE:55==
5.2. Measuring equipment
After the coordinates from each movement were registered, a linear regression was made for each
reading where the opening angle was plotted as a function of the coordinates; see figure 21 for
x-coordinates, figure 22 for y-coordinates, and figure 23 for z-coordinates. The coordinates from
the opening angle of 10◦were not included in the regression due to imprecision compared to the
other angles.
Figure 21: Linear regression, X-reading
Figure 22: Linear regression, Y-reading
41 of 253
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 5 | Method
Figure 23: Linear regression, Z-reading
As illustrated in figure 21, 22, and 23, only the z-coordinates gave a good trend line with an
acceptable precision. Therefore, the z-coordinates for this tag were preferential for extracting the
opening angle of the door.
During the calibration of the compass tag in the home of Nathalie Francisca Henriksen, it was
seen that the tag did not always provide the end position of the door. This happened when the
movement was either too slow or discontinuous. The compass tag then detected the coordinates
on the way to the end position. This was taken into account when calibrating the compass tags
in the dwellings by ensuring a smooth and rapid movement. However, this was a source of error
since the occupants in the dwellings did not necessarily open or close their windows as rapid and
smooth.
Since the calibration method showed a clear consistency between at least one coordinate and
opening angle, the same procedure was used to calibrate each tag on the windows in the test
dwellings.
The calibration of the compass tags in the dwellings took place from the 15th of
September 2017 to 23rd of October 2017. However, the calibration was performed with a fewer
number of angles (fully open, half open, and approximately 10-15◦) in order to optimize the work
flow at the site. Additionally, the sensitivity was checked during the calibration in order to ensure
that the correct sensitivity was chosen for each compass tag so that small openings also were
detected.
During the calibration, some tags were moved from one window to another, moved from one corner
of the window to another, etc. This was done due to e.g. lack of new compass tags where the
researchers in some cases evaluated that a tag was better utilized in e.g. a kitchen or living room
where the Netatmos collected data. Tags were also moved if they were placed too close to other
magnetic sensors.
42 of 253
==PAGE:57==

Netatmo
Netatmo was the equipment used to measure indoor and outdoor climate parameters and was
connected to the Wi-Fi in the respective dwellings. The Netatmo equipment will be described in
