5.2.2.1
Indoor climate
Each dwelling was equipped with three Netatmo modules; a main indoor module, an outdoor
module, and an additional indoor module, see figure 24.
Figure 24: Netatmo modules (left to right: Main indoor, outdoor, additional indoor) From [30]
The large indoor module was the main module; it was connected to WiFi and had to be connected
to power in order for the system to be online. The other two modules were connected to the main
module via WiFi and ran on battery. As its name indicates, the outdoor module was originally
meant to be used outdoors. However, in this project it was used indoors since the outdoor data
was common for the whole area and was collected by one set of measuring instruments.
Depending on the type of module, the number of parameters they were able to measure differed.

In regards to CO2-concentration, the Netatmo modules continuously calibrated themselves in fixed
intervals. It assumed that the CO2-concentration reached a minimum level of 400 ppm during the
previous week of the calibration and hereby adjusted itself for further measurements [31].
The main indoor module was placed in the room which the occupants used the most when they
were awake. This was known by asking the participants [5]. The main indoor module was most
frequently placed in the living room, the additional indoor module was then placed in the bedroom,
and the outdoor module was placed in an office or elsewhere in the dwelling. This placement was
done due to the number of parameters that each module is able to measure as shown in table
4.
The modules had a recording frequency of 5 minutes and an offline storage with the capacity
of logging data for a week [5]. The data from all modules was loaded to an online database on
my.netatmo.com. Since May 2017, all participants were able to follow their own indoor climate
conditions in detailed diagrams. An example of the CO2-distribution during a day is presented in
figure 25.

on the module lit either green, yellow, or red depending on the level of CO2 in the room. The
threshold values of when the module light changed color is shown in figure 26 [30].

5.2.3
Maintenance of measuring equipment
The research project took place over a period of approximately one year and it was therefore
necessary to continuously maintain the sensors in the dwellings. The authors of this thesis took
over the maintenance in the period from the 15th September 2017 to the 4th December 2017. Since
the sensors were already installed, the maintenance phase mostly consisted of changing batteries
of all compass tags, PIR and Reed KumoSensors, and the small Netatmo modules as well as
reconnecting the Netatmos and ethernet tag managers which had lost the connection.
Since the accelerometer tags were neglected, the intention was to install new compass tags on the
windows with previously installed accelerometer tags during the maintenance. This was, however,
only done for a few windows due to the financial limitations of the research project. Many windows
in the dwellings were not equipped with a compass tag and could therefore not be included in the
window operation models.
5.3
Data handling
The data from all measuring equipment was collected and gathered in the software program R; a
program used for statistical computing [32]. The goal of the data handling process was to achieve
data tables containing data from all measuring devices. For each dwelling, two data tables were
made; one table for deriving both window opening and closing model, and one table for deriving
the opening angle model. The data was gathered the 4th of December 2017 resulting in models
based on data from approximately 9-12 months.
See specific time frame for each dwelling in
Appendix C. In order to achieve these combined data tables, a lot of data handling was necessary.
The overall steps in the data handling are illustrated in the flowchart in figure 29.
46 of 253
==PAGE:61==
5.3. Data handling
Figure 29: Flowchart of data handling process leading to the three types of models
This section will describe the steps of the data handling process; cleaning and merging data as
well as the logic behind setting up explanatory variables such as time of day, season, room type,
and presence identification.
5.3.1
Cleaning data
This section describes how the data from the wireless sensor tags was cleaned. The Netatmo data
did not undergo a cleaning process since everything was in the form it should be.
First of all, the data from respectively the compass tags, PIR and Reed Kumosensors were sepa-
rated from each other. Afterwards, the next step for all three sensor tag types was cleaning up the
datasets. The main indicators used to separate the tags from each other were the dwelling name
and the specific tag ID which each tag automatically was given by the wireless sensor system.
An important matter for the sensor tags was making each and every tag unique and consistent
throughout the entire dataset. The overall cleaning of data consisted of the following steps:
47 of 253
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 5 | Method
1. Temperature and RH updates: The sensor tags measured temperature and relative
humidity every second to fifth minute. This data was not relevant in the final models in
this study, and was deleted from the dataset. The temperature and RH from the Netatmo
modules was used instead since they better indicated the overall indoor parameters of the
respective rooms.
2. Dwelling names: There was inconsistency in how the dwellings had been named in the
URL-calling.
In order to create coherence and hereby use the data more efficiently, all
inconsistent dwelling names were renamed.
3. Tag ID: Some tags had been replaced during the experiment since they stopped responding.
For PIR and Reed KumoSensors, these tags were renamed so that the tag ID for a specific
tag position was consistent throughout the entire research project.
For compass tags, a
calibration was performed on the old placement as well as on the new placement. In order
to use the calibration regression from both placements, it was necessary to distinguish the
tag from old measurements to future measurements. This was done by giving the tag two
different ID’s; one for the old scenario and one for the new scenario.
4. Test data: When testing the measuring equipment, the researchers test data infiltrated
with the actual data. All data from testing periods was removed from the dataset.
5. Nonparticipating dwellings: Only the dwellings which were participating in the research
project in the time frame of this master thesis were integrated in the window operation mod-
els. The reason was that only the data from dwellings where compass tags were calibrated
could be used. This meant that data from nine dwellings was extracted from the dataset.
6. Calibration date and time: The time period of approximately two hours where the
calibration of compass tags took place in each dwelling was deleted in the dataset, since the
openings and closings of windows in this time period were not representative of the occupant
behaviour in the dwellings.
7. Deleting data from compass tags: Some compass tags were not able to be calibrated due
to water damage, being out of range, etc. Any data from these tags was therefore deleted
from the dataset.
The data from the compass tags went through a more detailed preparation than the PIR and
Reed Kumosensors due to the need for incorporating the linear regressions from the calibrations,
calculating the opening angles, and checking the results hereof.
The calibration regressions for each tag were inserted and the opening angles were calculated
for each movement detection.
The linear regression with the best R2-value (either x-reading,
y-reading, or z-reading) for each tag was chosen and assigned to the respective tag.
The liability of the calculated opening angles was afterwards checked. This was done by assign-
ing the maximum opening angle to each window.
Hereby, it was possible to see whether the
48 of 253
==PAGE:63==
5.3. Data handling
calculated opening angles were below the maximum possible opening angle. The liability of the
calculated opening angles were likewise checked by seeing if any angles were notably lower than
0◦(=closed).
Unfortunately, it was found for many tags that the calibration regressions only were valid for a
specific period of time. The angles fluctuated largely over time and some observations from the
tags were way above the maximum opening angle and way below 0◦. Scatter plots were made in
the hope of finding a coherence between the opening angle and an influential parameter which
then could be used to correct the angles continuously. The following relations were investigated:
• Tag temperature vs. opening angle
• Battery voltage vs. opening angle
• Tag temperature vs. reading
• Battery voltage vs. reading
The temperature used for the plots was the temperature measured by the tag itself since the
temperature on the window glass got very high during sunny summer hours and very cold during
winter compared to the general room temperature.
The battery voltage which was logged at
my.wirelesstag.net for each tag was used. Furthermore, the reading providing the highest R2 was
used for this investigation. As an example of these plots, two compass tags were selected to be
presented in the thesis.
Figure 30 presents the scatter plot of temperature vs.

There was not found any coherence or system between the fluctuations over time of the different
tags. Therefore, it was necessary to adjust the angles of each tag manually. The tag observations
were partitioned into time intervals and afterwards either added or subtracted the necessary
number of degrees. This was done by looking at the angle plots. Taking figure 34a as an example,
the observations were split into two time intervals; 1) observations before the 1st of August and 2)
observations after the 1st of August (respectively before and after the calibration). The angles in
the first interval were added with 35◦and the angles in the second interval were subtracted with
5◦. Hereafter, all angles below 4◦were set equal to zero. The latter was done for all compass tags
because the measuring equipment was imprecise and, therefore, an opening angle of 4◦would in
most cases actually be equal to a closing movement. The results of the adjustment for the same

5.3.2
Merging and preparing data
After all data was cleaned and angles were corrected, the next step was to merge all data into large
tables in R, set up conditions for occupant presence, and prepare the data tables for logistic and
linear regressions. The data tables contained different parameters depending on which model type
was derived. The final data table for window opening and closing models contained the following
variables from which GLMs could be derived:
53 of 253
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 5 | Method
• Date and time of the experiment in 5 minute intervals as well as time of day categorization
and season categorization
• Dwelling name to identify the behavioural pattern through
• Indoor climate data
• Outdoor climate data
• Identification of whether a window was opened or not
• Identification of whether a window was closed or not
• Identification of the window state (open/closed)
• Categorized room type in which windows were located
• Categorized presence identification
The models which provided the opening angle were made as LMs. In order to make the large
combined data table for this purpose, the data table was further developed so that it contained
the following variables:
• Date and time of the experiment in 5 minute intervals as well as time of day categorization
and season categorization
• Dwelling name to identify the behavioural pattern through
• Indoor climate data
• Outdoor climate data
• Opening angle
• Categorized room type in which windows were located
• Categorized presence identification
The steps in the process of making these data tables were as follows:
1. Time step: The time step of the data table controlled the time interval of the probability
prediction. Since the time step of Netatmo observations was approximately 5 minutes, it
allowed deriving models predicting the probability of a window operation happening within
the next 5 minutes. The large data table was therefore built in a 5 minute time interval from
the beginning of the experiment to the end of the data collecting in this master thesis (4th
of December 2017). Data from Netatmo indoor equipment, Netatmo outdoor equipment,
compass tags, PIR and Reed Kumosensors was loaded into this time step if there was an
observation within the respective 5 minutes. It was modelled to take the next 5 minutes
into account and then choose the last detection if there were multiple detections within the
5 minutes. This is illustrated in figure 36.
54 of 253
==PAGE:69==
5.3. Data handling
Figure 36: Illustration of inserting data in time steps
Since the wireless sensor tags could detect every tenth second, some of the information was
naturally lost when transforming the data to a 5 minute interval. It was, however, necessary
in order to link all the data together. Furthermore, it was assumed to be acceptable not to
have all detections for window operations within 5 minutes since the indoor climate most
likely would not be affected drastically if a window was opened once or twice during a 5
minute period. Therefore, a time step of 5 minutes was still considered reasonable.
2. Season: In order to see whether the season had an influence on the occupants’ interaction
with windows, the following season categorizations were made:
• March-May: Spring
• June-august: Summer
• September-November: Fall
• December-February: Winter
3. Time of day: In order to see whether the time of day had an influence on the occupants’
interaction with windows, the following time categorizations were made:
• 00:00:00-05:59:59: Night
• 06:00:00-11:59:59: Morning
• 12:00:00-17:59:59: Afternoon
• 18:00:00-23:59:59: Evening
4. Room type: In order to see whether the room type had an influence on the occupants’
interaction with windows, the following room categorizations were made:
• Livingroom
• Kitchen
• Mixed kitchen/livingroom
• Bedroom
• Others (office, entrance, hall, etc.)
5. Link between compass tags and Netatmo modules: In order to use the indoor envi-
ronmental variables for deriving the window operation models, it was necessary to determine
55 of 253
==PAGE:70==
Chapter 5 | Method
which compass tags were in rooms with which Netatmo modules. If a compass tag was in
the same room as a Netatmo module, it was linked to the specific module. In the large
data table, three extra columns (CO2, temperature, and humidity) were made for indoor
climate of rooms without a Netatmo module. The input for these columns was an average
between the observations of the other modules in the residence. Compass tags in rooms
without Netatmo modules were thus linked to this data. Additionally, the outdoor Netatmo
module used for indoor purposes could not measure CO2. Therefore, an assumption was
made that the room with this module had a CO2-level equal to the average of the main and
the additional indoor module.
6. Window opening and closing: For each window, it was noted when a window operation
happened within the 5 minute time interval. The notation differentiated between window
closings and window openings.
7. Window status: When a window stood open, it could be either opened more, opened
less, or closed. Contrary, when the window was closed, it could only be opened. Therefore,
identification of the window status (open/closed) was made so that the model calculating
the probability of a window closing only was calculated based on data where the window
was not already closed.
8. Entrance door movements: In general, it did not take 5 minutes for an occupant to
leave the dwelling. If a door was opened and closed within the same 5 minutes, only one
of the movements would be included in the table. This was misleading when wanting to
predict occupancy. Therefore, the reed detections were manipulated as such; if the door
was opened and then closed within 5 minutes, the detection was marked as moved. If the
door was opened without a closing within the next five minutes, the detection was marked
opened. If the door was closed (the action) without an opening in the previous five minutes,
the detection was marked closed.
9. Occupant presence: The probability of a window action was only calculated if there was
presence in the dwelling. Therefore, it was necessary to set up conditions for when occupants
were assumed to be present or not. The conditions were:
• The door stood open (the state): If the door stood open it was assumed that there was
presence regardless if there was a PIR detection or not. This condition accounted until
the door state changed.
• The door was either closed (the action) or moved: If the door was moved/closed some-
one could either have entered the house or left the house. Only if there was a PIR
detection between a moved/closed Reed detection and the next Reed detection, it was
assumed that there was occupancy.
• Window movements: Theoretically, it should also be assumed that there was occupancy
if a window changed its state. However, this was not included as an assumption in this
thesis since the sensitivity of the compass tags during parts of fall was so high that
56 of 253
==PAGE:71==
5.3. Data handling
observations could occur at all times.
10. Occupant presence status: In order to include presence status, the following categoriza-
tions were made:
• Arriving: The first 10 minutes after the occupant arrived to the house were assigned
with the occupant status arriving.
• Departing: The last 10 minutes before the occupant left the house were assigned with
the occupant status departing.
• Intermediate: Observations between arriving and departing were assigned with the
occupant status intermediate.
• Short stay: Some observations showed that the occupant could arrive and leave again
within a very short time period. If this time period was less than 20 minutes, the
occupant status within this time frame was called short stay.
This presence status was applied every time there was a door movement (Reed detection)
if the occupant presence algorithm assumed occupancy after the door movement. There-
fore, the presence status did not take into account whether an additional occupant entered
the dwelling or an occupant already being present just moved the door without leaving or
entering the building.
11. Data gaps: When the Netatmo modules were offline, out of battery, etc. it resulted in
data gaps. Likewise, the wireless sensor tags would stop responding and saving observations
offline if the ethernet tag manager was offline for too long. When calculating the GLMs and
LMs in R, no data gaps were allowed. Therefore, it was necessary to consider how these data
gaps should be handled. Some data gaps were larger than others and they were therefore
handled differently. The different scenarios were as follows:
• If there was a data gap less than or equal to 10 minutes for the Netatmo modules, the
data from the previous observation was copied. This was assumed to be a reasonable
assumption since the indoor climate most likely would not change drastically within 10
minutes. The same assumption accounted for the outdoor climate data.
• There were also scenarios for the Netatmo modules where e.g. the main module and
the indoor module had observations but the outdoor module did not. In such cases,
average values from the remaining module(s) were used to fill in the gaps.
• If there were gaps larger than 10 minutes with missing Netatmo data from all modules,
the gap was deleted completely. This was e.g. the case for many dwellings in July
and/or August where no students maintained the equipment. Therefore, parts of these
months were in many cases excluded from the models.
• If there were data gaps from wireless sensor tags for Reed, PIR, and compass tags, the
periods were still included. If the window e.g. stood open when the tag got offline,
the window would appear open during the offline periods. This was both in regards
57 of 253
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 5 | Method
to a single tag being offline and the entire wireless sensor tag system being offline in a
dwelling. This will be discussed in 7 Discussion.
5.3.3
Transforming data table
When the data was combined in the large data tables, it was done as a horizontal table meaning
that multiple columns were made for the same explanatory variables if multiple measurements
took place. This was the case for indoor climate parameters since they were measured in multiple
rooms. Therefore, indoor temperature, indoor CO2-concentration, indoor RH, and the respec-
tive room type were each registered in four different columns; one for main, indoor, outdoor,
and no Netatmo module. In addition, observations made on the windows were represented in
separate columns per compass tag. Contrary to this, the variables time of day, season, presence
status, and outdoor climate parameters were only represented once in the data table since multiple
observations/measurements did not take place.
When deriving a model, multiple columns representing the same explanatory variables were not
allowed. Therefore, it had to be considered how to combine these columns into one column. This
could be done by 1) stacking the columns on top of each other or 2) merging the columns into one
column (e.g. by taking average values). When stacking columns on top of each other it resulted
in a vertically longer table with more rows than before. It also resulted in the columns for time
of day, season, presence status, and outdoor climate parameters having empty cells. In order to
derive a model, no empty cells were allowed. Therefore, it required copying data from the same
time steps and inserting it in the empty cells. An example of this is illustrated in figure 37. The
red marked cells indicate data that was copied.
Figure 37: Example of stacking columns and copying data
When copying data, overrepresentation occurred since the same data appeared more than once.
Hereby, a risk of the copied data having a larger influence on the derived models than usual arose.
Assumptions regarding copying of data was carefully considered and kept at a minimum level
since it could lead towards false estimations.
In regards to Netatmo indoor climate measurements, the only possible way to keep a difference
58 of 253
==PAGE:73==
5.3. Data handling
between indoor climate in the different rooms was to stack the Netatmo indoor climate data
columns on top of each other. Hereby, copying of outdoor climate, time, and presence related
data was required up to four times depending on the dwelling.
When looking at the columns representing window openings and window closings, these were also
only allowed once per Netatmo module regardless of the number of windows in each room. When
having only one Netatmo module in a room, it was considered how to handle the data when there
was more than one window in the same room. The following two options were considered:
1. Option 1: The windows could be inserted separately and stacked on top of each other by
using the same data for the other explanatory variables multiple times. Hereby, each window
would be included separately in the model. The risk of overrepresenting the explanatory
variables in this option would increase drastically since it would require data to be copied
once for every time an additional window was monitored in a room.
2. Option 2: The windows could be merged per Netatmo module into one column for window
opening and one column for window closing representing all windows in the respective room
as one combined window. This could also be done in rooms without a Netatmo module.
Hereby, the model would not take into account whether several windows were opened at the
same time in the same room. The amount of data required to be copied would decrease
compared to option 1 but be the same as when stacking the Netatmo data.
In order to reduce the risk of overrepresenting data, the second option was used to handle multiple
windows in a room when deriving the models. The data table for each dwelling was transformed
from a horizontal to a vertical data table as illustrated in figure 38.
59 of 253
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 5 | Method
Figure 38: Illustration of data table transformation and copying of data
As illustrated in figure 38 with the boxes in different green shades, data linked to the Netatmo
modules was only inserted once since multiple measurements occurred. The overrepresented ex-
planatory variables time of day, season, outdoor climate, and presence status could influence the
derived models wrongly as the data was copied and inserted several times. This was, however,
required in order to link the Netatmo data to the respective compass tags and distinguish between
indoor climate in different rooms. The influence of the overrepresentation of data will be further
discussed in 7 Discussion.
5.4
Modelling of window operation
Modelling of window operations in the dwellings consisted of the following three models for each
dwelling:
60 of 253
==PAGE:75==
5.4. Modelling of window operation
• Window openings: A model predicting the probability of any window in a specific room
being opened during the next 5 minutes. The model was derived from the entire transformed
data table since it was assumed that a window could be opened more or less even though
it already stood open. The model was derived as a GLM by using logistic regression with
forward and backward selection and AIC as selection criteria.
• Window closings: A model predicting the probability of any window in a specific room
being closed during the next 5 minutes. The model was derived from the part of the trans-
formed data table where a window already stood open since a closed window cannot close
again.
The model was derived as a GLM by using logistic regression with forward and
backward selection and AIC as selection criteria.
• Opening angle: A model predicting the opening angle of a window opening. The model
was based on the entire transformed data table for opening angles and should be used when
a window opening is predicted by the window opening model. The model was derived as
an LM by using basic linear regression with forward and backward selection and AIC as
selection criteria.
The three model types were derived based on data containing the same possible explanatory
variables. These are seen in table 7 where the type of explanatory variable is marked.
Table 7: Type of explanatory variables

During the modelling stage it was found that 1) the data table for one of the dwellings had too
few inputs in order to derive the models, and 2) the equipment from another dwelling did not
work properly. These two dwellings were therefore discarded in the modelling stage. Due to lack
of data, two additional dwellings did not allow deriving all three kinds of models. Hereby, the
modelling of window operation resulted in 35 window opening models, 34 window closing models,
and 33 opening angle models.
61 of 253
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 5 | Method
In all models, interaction terms were made between explanatory variables.
Interaction terms
means that some explanatory variables might have a different influence when interacting with
other explanatory variables. Interaction terms were included for presence status combined with
all continuous explanatory variables. Presence status was assumed to be of high importance since
occupants due to adaption more likely open windows when arriving rather than after being in
the same place for several hours even though the conditions would be the same. Therefore, the
same temperature and CO2-level might cause different actions depending on the presence status.
Interaction terms were not made between categorical variables due to the complexity of evaluating
the outcome of such interaction terms.
When deriving the models, R gave the warning message ”glm.fit: fitted probabilities numerically
0 or 1 occurred” in some of the final GLMs. The warning message could be due to a too small
dataset for the given dwelling. The message did not mean that the model was not valid. However,
an evaluation of such model should be done carefully. Table 8 gives an overview of the models
containing this warning message.
Table 8: Warning message in R in final GLM

5.4.1
Model checking
After deriving the models, an investigation of the model fit based on the theory in 2.1.3.4 Checking
the fit of the models was made for each model. The residual plots were quite similar for all window
opening and closing models and, therefore, only an example from one dwelling is presented here.
In regards to the opening angle model, the residuals differed more. The worst and best model fit
will therefore be presented here.
Linearity
The assumption of linearity was checked by looking at the patterns of data points on a residual
vs. predicted/fitted values plot. The residual plots of the three models; window opening, window
closing, and opening angle are illustrated in figure 39.
62 of 253
==PAGE:77==
5.4. Modelling of window operation
(a) Window opening model
(b) Window closing model
(c) Opening angle model (worst fit)
(d) Opening angle model (best fit)
Figure 39: Example plots with residuals vs. predicted/fitted values before variable transformation
In the plots for the GLMs for both window opening and window closing, see figure 39a and 39b,
the data points formed two straight lines. Since the data points formed a certain pattern in this
kind of plot, the assumption of linearity was violated. The residual plots of these two models
could indicate that an unknown variable might have been missing in the models. Finding the
unknown variable could be a solution for remedying the non-linearity.
In regards to the LM for opening angles, the situation was quite different.
As seen in figure
63 of 253
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 5 | Method
39c, the data points show a linear tendency and the linearity assumption is therefore violated.
Contrary, figure 39d, shows no pattern in the data points in the plot. The assumption of linearity
of this model was therefore complied. Whether the linearity assumption was violated or complied
differed from dwelling to dwelling when speaking of the opening angle models.
Homoscedasticity
The assumption of homoscedasticity was also evaluated based on the plots in figure 39. For both
the window opening and window closing models, heteroscedasticity occurred. This was concluded
since 1) the upper data point line had remarkably larger residuals than the lower line, and 2) the
magnitude of the residuals differed in both data point lines leading to larger residuals in one end
than the other. Hence, the data was not equally described by the model. For the opening angle
models, there was a difference between the worst and the best fit. The model with the worst fit
showed heteroscedasticity with smaller residuals occurring for lower values and larger residuals for
higher values. Contrary, the model with the best fit was homoscedastisc. Hence, the assumption
of homoscedasticity was violated in all closing and opening models but only in some of the opening
angle models.
Normality of residuals
The assumption of normality of the residuals was checked by a normal Q-Q plot. The plots for
the three models; window opening, window closing, and opening angle for the chosen dwellings
are illustrated in figure 40.
64 of 253
==PAGE:79==
5.4. Modelling of window operation
(a) Window opening model
(b) Window closing model
(c) Opening angle model (worst fit)
(d) Opening angle model (best fit)
Figure 40: Examples of normal Q-Q plots of residuals before variable transformation
As seen in figure 40a and 40b, the window opening and window closing models were far from
having normally distributed residuals since the data points did not form a combined straight line.
Again, the two separate lines in the plot could indicate a missing explanatory variable. Contrary
to these two model types, the fit of the opening angle model varied from dwelling to dwelling.
The dwelling having the worst fit did not contain normally distributed residuals since the slope
of the data point line varied, see figure 40c. The dwelling having the best fit showed a tendency
of normality of the residuals, see figure 40d. However, improvements could still be done.
65 of 253
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 5 | Method
Since the normal Q-Q plot revealed that none of the models had residuals with perfectly bell-
shaped normal distributions, an evaluation of the data behind the explanatory variables was
made. This was done by plotting histograms of all the climate parameters. If the climate parame-
ters were symmetrically distributed, it was an indication that the residuals for this parameter were
normally distributed. The histograms showed that most of the explanatory variable measurements
had symmetric distributions. However, indoor CO2-concentration, outdoor RH, wind strength,
and gust strength needed improvement, see figure 41a, 42a, 43a, and 44a. Therefore, several types
of transformations were tested for these variables in order to investigate which transformation
formulas provided the most symmetric distributions. The tested transformation formulas were

68 of 253
==PAGE:83==
5.4. Modelling of window operation
a more symmetric distribution after the transformation. Therefore, it was considered necessary
to transform these four explanatory variables in order to improve the model fit. The variable
transformations were included in the final model.
Absence of collinearity
It was assumed that none of the explanatory variables were collinear. An example of two theoreti-
cally collinear variables modelled as non-collinear variables was temperature and relative humidity.
The risk of assuming absence of collinearity would be that relative humidity e.g. would appear
in the model as explanatory variable without temperature even though the temperature might
actually be the variable which drove the occupant toward the action of e.g. opening the window.
The influence of temperature could therefore be undermined by relative humidity and vice versa.
This was therefore thoroughly considered under the interpretation of which explanatory variables
were included in the respective models, see 7 Discussion.
No influential data points
In order to prevent influential data points, data from the wireless sensor tags was checked for
evidential error measurements when cleaning the data. It was e.g. clear when a compass tag
provided a measurement error, since one of the x-, y-, or z-coordinates was exactly 0. These data
points were discarded. In regards to Netatmo data, no cleaning was done since no clear error
measurements were found. The outliers of the climate condition measurements could also act
as influential data points. Lastly, the instability of the measuring equipment might have caused
influential data points. It could therefore not be concluded whether influential data points still
occurred after the cleaning.
Independence
The subjects in the three types of models were the dwellings.
Therefore, the assumption of
independence was complied since each model only contained data from a single dwelling.
Conclusion of model fit
According to the model checking based on residuals, the window opening and closing models did
not have a good fit. Some of the opening angle models were better fitted but there was still room
for improvements. However, it should be kept in mind that the models were made to predict
occupant behaviour, which is a very complex situation. The checking method was therefore only
used as guide line to see where improvements were desired.

==PAGE:84==
Chapter 5 | Method
70 of 253
==PAGE:85==
6
Results
This chapter presents the results found in this master thesis. The measured data in the field
will firstly be presented followed by a presentation of the model fits as well as the intercepts and
coefficients of the final models. The liability of the measured data and models will be commented
on continuously throughout the result chapter.
6.1
Measured climate conditions in the field
The measured climate conditions in- and outside the dwellings will be presented here from the
beginning of the measurements in each individual dwelling to the 4th of December 2017 (the
first measurements started the 24th of November 2016). To see the specific time frame of the
experiment for each dwelling, see Appendix C. The measured values are presented as boxplots in
order to evaluate the median and dispersion.
The measured indoor temperatures in the 35 dwellings are presented in figure 45.
71 of 253
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 6 | Results
Figure 45: Measured indoor temperatures in the dwellings
Figure 45 shows that the median of the measured temperatures in the dwellings were between 20◦C
and nearly 25◦C. The dispersion of the measurements between the lower and upper measurement
excluding outliers differed from dwelling to dwelling. Dwelling 3 had a notable small dispersion
compared to the other dwellings followed by dwelling 13. The indoor temperature measurements
ranged from 13-29◦C excluding outliers and seemed as reasonable measurements. It was known
to the authors that several occupants slept with their windows open in their bedrooms all year
round which could be a reason for the lower part of the temperature measurements. Some of
the occupants were also very aware of their indoor environment and explained how they often
ventilated their rooms through window openings. The upper range of the measured temperature
could be due to warm periods when the occupants were not home, e.g. if they were away on
summer vacation or simply at work on a warm summer day.
They could also be due to the
Netatmos being placed with access to direct sunlight which would lead to false indications of the
air temperature.
For both lower and upper temperatures, a lot of outliers were found. The smallest lower outlier
was just below 5◦C in dwelling 19 and the largest upper outlier was nearly 35◦C in dwelling 20
and 32. However, 24 of the dwellings did not reach temperature measurements above 30◦C for
outliers. The extreme outliers were most likely due to errors in the measuring equipment.
The measured indoor relative humidities in the 35 dwellings are presented in figure 46.
72 of 253
==PAGE:87==
6.1. Measured climate conditions in the field
Figure 46: Measured indoor RH in the dwellings
When looking at figure 46, it is seen that the median RH varied from dwelling to dwelling. The
lowest median RH was found in dwelling 3 at 40% whereas the highest was in dwelling 30 at ap-
proximately 55%. Furthermore, the dispersion between lower and upper measurements excluding
outliers differed from dwelling to dwelling but only dwelling 3 had a remarkably small dispersion.
Across all dwellings, the RH-levels ranged from 19-77% excluding outliers. According to DS/EN
15251 [14], the recommended RH-levels for category III for dehumidification and humidification
are maximum 70% and minimum 20% respectively. The measured RH values in the dwellings
therefore seemed realistic as they were were close to this interval. Additionally, it was seen that
especially dwelling 8, 10, 15, and 30 had outliers for both lower and upper quartiles.
The measured indoor CO2-concentrations in the 35 dwellings are presented in figure 47.
73 of 253
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 6 | Results
Figure 47: Measured indoor CO2-concentrations in the dwellings
Figure 47 shows that the medians of the CO2-concentrations in the dwellings were within 500-
1125ppm. The low medians at approximately 500ppm could be realistic if occupants often venti-
lated the dwelling through window opening. However, the boxplots reveal that a relatively large
amount of the measured values below the lower quartile were unrealistically low since they were
lower than the outdoor CO2-concentration at 400ppm. The low CO2-measurements were perhaps
due to the fact that the measuring equipment automatically calibrated itself, see 5.2.2 Netatmo.
This could have influenced the models wrongly if indoor CO2 was included as explanatory variable
since window openings might seem to occur at lower CO2-concentrations than in reality.
Furthermore, figure 47 shows that the dispersion of the CO2-concentrations between lower and
upper value excluding outliers also differed widely between the dwellings. For almost all dwellings,
it was common that the measured CO2-concentrations had significantly large upper outliers. Some
of the outliers reached 5000ppm which was quite high compared to their upper quartiles.
The measured outdoor temperatures, outdoor relative humidities, and rain fall are presented in
figure 48, 49, and 50, respectively.

Figure 49: Measured outdoor

Figure 50: Measured rainfall in

Figure 48 shows an outdoor temperature range between -11◦C and 32◦C as well as a median
at 10◦C. The range of the measured outdoor temperatures showed that the models were made
based on both cold and warm periods representing a whole year. The average measured outdoor
temperature was calculated to 10◦C. According to DMI [13], the average temperature in 2017 in
the municipality of Copenhagen was 9.6◦C and hence the measured data seemed reliable.
Figure 49 shows that the outdoor relative humidity ranged between 41% and 100% with a median
at approximately 88%. The upper quartile was quite high around 96% meaning that 25% of the
RH measurements were between 96% and 100%. The RH for 2017 measured by DMI at the area
of Copenhagen and North Zealand ranged between 70-95% [12]. Hereby, the field measurements in
this research project seemed acceptable with 50% of the RH measurements in the range of 74-96%
(corresponding to the measurements between upper and lower quartile in figure 49). However,
most measurements below the lower quartile were lower than the lowest DMI measurements. The
lowest RH measurement in this field study excluding outliers was 41%.
In regards to the measured rain (including all types of precipitation), see figure 50, most mea-
surements were 0mm and all measurements above 0mm were included as outliers in the boxplot.
However, the sum of all rain measurements during the entire research project was 829mm. It
should therefore be kept in mind that measurements were made approximately every 5 minutes
for an entire year and hence most measurements were still 0mm even though a descent amount
of rain was measured. According to DMI, the total amount of precipitation in the municipality
75 of 253
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 6 | Results
of Copenhagen in 2017 was 805mm [13]. The measurements made in this field study therefore
seemed reliable.
For both wind and gust, the measured strengths and angles are presented in figure 51 and 52,
respectively.
Figure 51: Measured wind and gust strength in
the dwelling area
Figure 52: Measured wind and gust angle in
the dwelling area
Figure 51 shows that the wind strength was generally lower than the gust strength which is
consistent with reality. The median of wind strength was 7m/s and 14m/s for the gust strength.
The average measured wind strength was calculated to 7.4m/s in this study, and for all of Denmark
in 2017 it was 4.8m/s [13]. For Copenhagen and North Zealand the average wind strength per day
ranged between 2-9m/s [12], and in this study it ranged between 2-16m/s. The wind strengths in
this field study therefore seemed a bit too high. This could possibly be due to the wind gauge not
being able to distinguish between gust and wind.
Furthermore, figure 52 shows that the measured angles for both wind and gust were the same.
This made good sense since the direction of the gust would be the same as the wind direction.
In the municipality of Copenhagen, the wind angles in 2017 ranged from 0-359◦[12], and in this
study they ranged from 40-320◦. The wind angles did therefore not have as broad a range. To
compare the most common wind directions, a wind rose from Copenhagen Airport 1989-1999 from
DMI [26] was used, see figure 53. Unfortunately, it was not possible to find a wind rose with newer
76 of 253
==PAGE:91==
6.2. Measured window movements included in the models
data.
Figure 53: Wind rose, all year, Copenhagen Airport (1989-1999). From [26]
The wind rose shows that the most common wind angles in degrees from north were between 240◦
and 270◦for Copenhagen Airport. The median of the angle measurements in this experiment was
220◦which was close to the most common directions of the wind rose. The location of the dwelling
compound was also north of Copenhagen where the wind direction may have been a bit different
from Copenhagen Airport. By comparing the wind rose with the measurements from the wind
gauge, the wind angles were considered realistic. The few differences could also be due to obstacles
in the housing area as it was not measured on a bare field as at Copenhagen Airport.

As seen in table 10, the number of movements in the rooms differed widely. 1 registration was
the lowest for both openings and closings and 1075 and 5866 registrations were the highest for
openings and closings, respectively. In total, 100 different rooms were monitored (not taking into
consideration that others contained several rooms). In 72 out of the 100 rooms, the number of
closings was larger than openings. This could have been caused by 1) the sensitivities of the
compass tags being too high resulting in multiple closing measurements in a row, 2) the compass
tags registering closings due to discontinuous opening movements, 3) the angles being incorrectly
manipulated in the data handling phase, or 4) unstable measuring equipment. Contrary to this,
26 of the rooms had more openings than closings. This was most likely due to 1) the windows
being opened from one position to another, 2) the windows fluttering in the wind, 3) lacking of
closing measurements due to e.g. low sensitivity, or 4) unstable measuring equipment. In total,
there were 242% more closings than openings.
80 of 253
==PAGE:95==
6.2. Measured window movements included in the models
Figure 54 and 55 give an overview of how the total number of respectively window openings and
window closings were distributed in the different room types and seasons.
Figure 54: Distribution of window openings per room type and season
Figure 55: Distribution of window closings per room type and season
It would be presumed that the percentage of window openings per room type would be equal to
the percentage of closings per room type. However, figure 54 and 55 show that this was not the
case. This inequality was most likely due to 1) sensitivity settings of compass tags, or 2) the
windows being opened from one angle to another (in the cases where there were more openings
than closings). Figure 54 and 55 also show that most window operations occurred during the
season fall regardless of the room type. This could be due to a period in fall where many compass
tags had high sensitivities, or it could be due to window operations simply occurring most in
this transition season. In regards to room type, the majority of window operations happened
in the category bedroom with 34% of all openings and 42% of all closings. Since the room type
other contained many different room types, mixed kitchen/livingroom was most likely the room
type where second most window openings occurred with 17%. This made good sense since this
category covered two combined room types. However, it was odd that the lowest percentage of
closings was in this mixed kitchen/livingroom. When the rooms were separated in the dwellings,
81 of 253
==PAGE:96==
Chapter 6 | Results
more window openings took place in livingroom than in kitchen. Contrary, more closings took
place in kitchen than in livingroom.
Furthermore, it was of interest to see in which rooms the largest opening angles occurred. To get
an overview of how the opening angles for all dwellings were distributed per room type, boxplots
representing all measurements from dwelling 1-33 were made, see figure 56. Measurements from
dwelling 34 and 35 will not be included when speaking of opening angles since opening angle
models were not derived for these dwellings due to too small datasets. Since it also was of interest
to see whether season had an influence on the opening angles, this is also illustrated in figure 56.
To see the range of opening angles per room type per dwelling, see Appendix D.
Figure 56: Opening angles per room type and season
Figure 56 shows that both the median and largest measurement for opening angles differed per
82 of 253
==PAGE:97==
6.2. Measured window movements included in the models
room type. For the category all year and across all room types, the median ranged from 8◦to
28◦and the largest opening angle ranged from 110◦to 180◦. Generally, the opening angles for
bedroom were lower than for the other room types during the different seasons. This correlated
with many participants stating that they often used crack openings in their bedroom. The room
types including a kitchen generally had larger opening angles than livingroom and bedroom. This
made good sense due to a need of ventilating the room more quickly when cooking.
Season
influenced the opening angle such that larger opening angles occurred during summer and spring
than during winter and fall for all room types except mixed kitchen/livingroom. This seemed
reasonable and might be due to outdoor weather conditions.
The overall average opening angle as well as the average minimum and maximum opening angle
were calculated to 24◦, 6◦, and 78◦, respectively. However, the maximum opening angle differed
much from room type to room type.
This was influenced by the possible maximum opening
angle which differed widely due to many different window types with different sizes and opening
possibilities. The window type was, however, not included in the models.
It was also of interest to see how the durations of the window openings were related to the opening
angles. The opening durations were calculated based on the 5 minute time interval in the data
tables and are presented in boxplots in figure 57 for each room type divided in seasons. The
opening durations are calculated from a window opening to a window closing and do not take into
account if the opening angle was changed in between. Since the durations of a few window openings
were extremely large, possibly due to missing closing measurements, a zoom of the boxplots was
made so that it was possible to read the quartiles on the plot. Of all measurements, the largest
opening duration reached approximately 5750 hours corresponding to 240 days. However, the
maximum value excluding outliers was 66 hours. Therefore, the zoom was made for values up till
80 hours. In order to better read the medians, a zoom on these is also illustrated in the right side
of figure 57.
83 of 253
==PAGE:98==
Chapter 6 | Results
Figure 57: Zoom of opening durations per room type and season
Figure 57 shows that the dispersion during all year was largest for the room types other, again hav-
ing in mind that room type other included multiple room types. The median was approximately
0.5 hours for all room types all year meaning that 50% of all window openings lasted maximum
30 minutes. However, when looking at each room type per season, it is seen that the median of
especially bedroom spring and livingroom summer reached approximately 7 hours. Apart from
livingroom, bedroom had the longest opening durations during spring, summer, and winter. This
made good sense since many participants mentioned that they slept with their windows on the
theft protection hinge at night. However, the large opening durations for namely bedroom spring
(upper quartile = 17h), livingroom summer (upper quartile = 26.5h), and livingroom winter (upper
quartile = 36.5h) reached such high durations that they seemed questionable.
Figure 56 and 57 show that both opening angle and duration differed widely with season; hence
season had an influence on how the windows were opened.

6.2.1
Window operation in relation to indoor climate measurements
In this section the results of when windows were opened and closed in regards to the indoor climate
will be presented. It was investigated how many percentage of the registered window openings
and closings laid within the indoor climate categories of DS/EN 15251 [14].
To evaluate window operation behaviour in regards to indoor temperature, the percentage of
respectively measured openings and measured closings happening within the categories for indoor
temperature from DS/EN 15251 was investigated.
See table 11 and 12 for window operation
within respectively the heating and cooling season. The percentages are calculated on the basis of
all window movements for the entire year. Thus, the percentages in table 11 and 12 together add
up to 100%. It must be mentioned that the tables compare operative temperature with a mixture
between radiant temperature and air temperature which therefore led to some uncertainties.

Table 11 shows that most of window openings and closings happened within category I in the
heating season. Thus, window openings happened mostly when the indoor air temperature was
above 21◦C in the heating season. This seemed reasonable since it was presumed that occupants
did not open their windows as much if it seemed cold inside. However, it was notable that most
of the window closings in the heating season also happened when the indoor temperatures were
above 21◦. This was unexpected as it would be presumed that ventilating the room would lead
to decreasing indoor temperatures. A reason for this result could be due to the high sensitivity
on many compass tags, which resulted in several closing measurements even though the window
was already closed. Another reason could be short opening durations.

As seen in table 14, 47.2% of all window openings occurred when the CO2-concentration in the

project like this may be more aware of indoor climate than others, due to e.g. the feedback period
of Anne Sloth Bidstrups thesis [5], and might therefore have a tendency to open windows at
lower CO2-concentrations than others. Otherwise, it could be that there were other drivers than
CO2-concentration that drove the occupants towards actions.
6.3
Measured presence in the dwellings
Since the window operation models only were calculated on data when there was presence in
the dwellings, it was of interest to see whether the presence predictions based on the PIR and
Reed measurements were realistic. Figure 60 presents the percentage of presence in each dwelling
during the time frame of the experiment (excluding periods where measuring equipment was
87 of 253
==PAGE:102==
Chapter 6 | Results
offline). Furthermore, it shows the distribution of the four different presence statuses.
Figure 60: Presence percentage pr. dwelling including presence status
Figure 60 shows that the presence percentage in the dwellings differed between 49% and 95%
with dwelling 3 having the lowest presence percentage and dwelling 25 having the highest. To
evaluate the presence percentages, the following example was used: If a resident leaves the house
for work Monday to Friday at 07:00 and returns at 18:00, and stays home all weekend, the presence
percentage will be 67%. 21 out of the 35 dwellings had a presence percentage above 70%. Still, this
was not unreasonable since many of the occupants in the experiment were retired and therefore did
not go to work. Additionally, most dwellings had several occupants which lead to higher presence
percentages than the given example if occupants e.g. were not home at the same time. Thus, the
presence percentages were considered reasonable.
Looking at the distribution of the presence statuses during occupancy, it is seen that intermediate,
as assumed, was the largest.
All dwellings included the presence status short stay where the
occupant(s) only were present in the dwelling for maximum 20 minutes before they left again.
The distribution between arriving and departing were equally distributed in each dwelling, as
assumed; the amount of arrivals should be equal to the amount of departures. The distribution
of presence statuses was therefore also considered reasonable.
A limitation in the prediction of presence was that it was not possible to distinguish between the
number of occupants being home. Also, it was not possible to see whether a new occupant arrived
or whether the main door was just moved.
88 of 253
==PAGE:103==
6.4. Fit of final models
6.4
Fit of final models
The fit of the final window operation models were checked by residual plots. The residual plots
for the window opening and closing models were similar for all dwellings and, therefore, only one
example will be shown in this section. The residual plots for the window opening angle models,
however, differed much from dwelling to dwelling. Hence, the worst and best case will be presented
in this section.
Residual plots from the final window opening model of dwelling 26 are presented in figure 61.
(a) Residuals vs. predicted values
(b) Normal Q-Q plot
Figure 61: Residual plots of final window opening model, dwelling 26
Residual plots from the final window closing model of dwelling 20 are presented in figure 62.
89 of 253
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 6 | Results
(a) Residuals vs. predicted values
(b) Normal Q-Q plot
Figure 62: Residual plots of final window closing model, dwelling 20
As seen in figure 61 and 62, the window opening models and window closing models had similar
residual plots. Even though the most critical variables were transformed, cf. 5.4 Modelling of
window operation, the residuals vs. predicted values plot and the normal Q-Q plot still looked
the same as before transformation of variables. It could therefore be concluded that the linearity
assumptions for the final window opening models and window closing models still were violated
and that the residuals were not normally distributed. This accounted for the opening and closing
models for all dwellings. A way of remedying the model fit problems could be investigating whether
there were missing explanatory variables in the models. Suggestions for missing variables will be
given in 7.5 Further work.
The residual plots from the final window opening angle models with the best and the worst fit are
presented in 63.
90 of 253
==PAGE:105==
6.4. Fit of final models
(a) Residuals vs. fitted values (worst fit)
(b) Residuals vs. fitted values (best fit)
(c) Normal Q-Q plot (worst fit)
(d) Normal Q-Q plot (best fit)
Figure 63: Residual plots of final window opening models with worst and best fit
As seen in figure 63a, the window opening angle model for dwelling 31 resulted in a residual vs.
fitted values plot with a linear tendency. This indicated a violation of the linearity assumption.
Furthermore, the model showed heteroscedasticity since the residuals were smaller for lower fitted
values than for higher fitted values.
The model with the best residuals vs.
fitted plot had
data points closer to the desired blob-like shape and did not have heteroscedastic tendencies, see
figure 63b. It was therefore concluded that a linear model better described the angle model for
dwelling 10 than for dwelling 31. Of all opening angle models, 29 models showed clear signs of
heteroscedasticity.
In regards to the normal Q-Q plots, see figure 63c and 63d, the opening angle models with
respectively the worst and best normal distribution of residuals were for dwelling 14 and dwelling
91 of 253
==PAGE:106==
Chapter 6 | Results
9. The Q-Q plot for dwelling 14 was not a straight line as desired which meant that the residuals
were not normally distributed. On the other hand, the model for dwelling 9 had an almost perfect
normal distribution of residuals. Of all opening angle models, 3 models showed almost perfect
normal distributions of residuals.
For the opening angle models, it was generally found that the model fits were better than for the
window opening and closing models. Even the angle model with the worst fit was better than the
fits of the opening and closing models. However, there was still room for improvement of the fit
of opening angle models.
6.5
Final models
This section contains the final models made in this master thesis. Firstly, the specific intercepts
and coefficients for each derived model are presented in separate tables. Due to the number of
models and size of tables, only the three model types for dwelling 33 will be presented here as
an example. Intercept and coefficient tables for all other dwellings are attached in Appendix E.
Secondly, to get an overview of the models, results comparing the correlation of coefficients (pos-
itive or negative) will be presented. Finally, the most included and most influential explanatory
variables in the models will be presented.
Table 15, 16, and 17 present the intercepts and coefficients for respectively the window opening,
window closing, and opening angle models for dwelling 33.
92 of 253

As seen in table 15, 16, and 17, the included explanatory variables for dwelling 33 differed per
model type.
When looking at table 15, it is seen that 192 intercepts occurred. These intercepts represented
the categorical explanatory variables included in the window opening model for dwelling 33. This
meant that 192 logistic regressions with different intercepts represented the window opening be-
haviour in this model. Season, time of day, room type, and presence status all influenced the
window opening behaviour in dwelling 33. The higher the intercept, the higher the probability of
a window opening. In regards to the coefficients in the lower part of the table, it is seen that both
continuous variables and interaction terms were included in the model. The coefficients define the
slope of the logistic regressions meaning that the same slopes were valid for all 192 regressions. For
dwelling 33, indoor temperature, indoor CO2, and outdoor temperature were influential towards
window opening and therefore included in the model. Additionally, it is seen that the indoor
CO2-concentration had a particular influence when interacting with the different categories of
presence status. When a coefficient is negative, it means that the probability of opening a window
decreases with an increased explanatory variable; the correlation between the variable and the
probability is negative. This was unexpectedly seen for the CO2-concentration in this model.
Contrary, having a positive coefficient/correlation, the probability of a window opening increases
when the explanatory variable increases. This was the case for both in- and outdoor temperature
in this model, as presumed. Furthermore, the p-values in table 15 describing the significance of
each included explanatory variable showed that all included variables except log(indoor CO2+1)
with interaction terms and presence status were statistically significant; below 0.05.
As marked in the header in table 15, the window opening model for dwelling 33 had a warning
message as described in 5.4 Modelling of window operation. When interpreting this model, it was
therefore kept in mind that the model was derived on a small amount of data.
Table 16 shows that less categorical explanatory variables were included in the window closing
model than in the window opening model for dwelling 33. For this dwelling, presence status and
season were influential towards the window closing behaviour and thus included in the model. Since
spring was not included in the data behind the model, only summer, fall, and winter occurred.
Hereby, 12 intercepts presented the behaviour in this model. Furthermore, indoor temperature,
indoor RH, indoor CO2, and gust angle influenced the probability of window closings in this
dwelling; having a positive correlation between the variable and the probability for the first climate
parameter, and a negative correlation for the latter three. All included variables were statistically
significant due to low p-values.
In regards to the opening angle model for dwelling 33, see table 17, the only categorical variable
included in the model was time of day and hence the opening angle differed depending on whether
a window was opened e.g. in the morning or evening hours. The continuous variables indoor
temperature, indoor RH, and log(wind strength + 1) influenced the opening angle behaviour in
96 of 253
==PAGE:111==
6.5. Final models
dwelling 33. As the case for the window closing model for dwelling 33, all included explanatory
variables were of statistical significance in the opening angle model.
In total for all dwellings, 97%, 99%, and 98% of the included explanatory variables were sta-
tistically significant for the window opening, window closing, and opening angle models, respec-
tively.
6.5.1
Comparison between window opening models
In order to compare the derived models to each other, the percentage of how many dwellings
included a respective explanatory variable in their window opening model was calculated. Figure
64 presents which explanatory variables were included in the 35 window opening models and
whether the coefficients had a positive or negative correlation with the probability.
Figure 64: Included explanatory variables in window opening models, dwelling 1-35
For the window opening models, see figure 64, the categorical explanatory variables season, time of
day, room type, and presence status occurred in many of the models. Season was the explanatory
variable occurring most frequently; it was included in 92% of the models.
In regards to the
continuous explanatory variables, the most frequently occurring drivers were indoor temperature,
outdoor temperature, log(indoor CO2+1), and indoor RH. 45% of the models included indoor
and/or outdoor temperature as drivers for window opening. Most of these models had a positive
correlation between the variable and the probability. This was presumable since high in- and
97 of 253
==PAGE:112==
Chapter 6 | Results
outdoor temperatures might lead to higher window opening probabilities in reality. An explanation
of the models with a negative correlation with outdoor temperature could be that in periods with
high outdoor temperatures, the opening durations might be longer and, hence, the probability of
several openings will decrease. Most of the models including log(indoor CO2+1) had a positive
correlation between the variable and the probability; higher CO2-concentrations increased the
probability of a window opening. In regards to indoor RH, most of the models including this
variable were included with a negative correlation; higher indoor RH led to decreasing probabilities
of window openings. Furthermore, only a few window opening models included interaction terms
between presence status and indoor temperature, indoor RH, log(indoor CO2+1), and (outdoor
RH)1/3, respectively. This indicated that the relation between presence status and the continuous
variables was not as important as presumed.
Thus, the most included window opening drivers were 1) season, 2) room type, and 3) time of
day.
Since the categorical variables were of such importance, it was interesting to evaluate the effect
of these variables on the probability of a window opening. This was done through figure 65 which
is a comparison between the models including the given categorical variable.
Figure 65: Influence of categorical drivers on window opening probability
Figure 65 is based on the intercepts; the higher the intercept, the higher the probability of a window
opening. Taking season as an example, 94% of the models including season as categorical driver,
had fall as the highest season-related intercept. Thus, in these models, fall was the season leading
to the highest window opening probability, which was highly in correlation with the measured
data presented in figure 54. The reason why all categorical drivers did not add up to 100% was
98 of 253
==PAGE:113==
6.5. Final models
that not all models included all types of categories; e.g. no models included all room types. In
59% of the models including season as driver, summer was the season leading to the lowest window
opening probabilities. In fact, none of the models had summer as the season leading to highest
probabilities. This was also in correlation with the measured data shown in figure 54. This could
be due to 1) the transition seasons spring and fall presumably having higher window opening
probabilities than summer since the opening durations during summer are longer, or 2) many
dwellings had offline periods during summer. The reason why fall had such high window opening
probabilities was that 1) fall is a transition season and 2) the majority of window openings were
measured during fall due to high sensitivities of the compass tags.
64% of the window opening models including time of day as driver had morning as the time of day
leading to the highest window opening probability. In all models, night was either contributing
to the lowest or next lowest window opening probability. These results were as presumed and
seemed rather reasonable.
Although arriving was presumed to be the presence status with the highest probability of window
openings, 62% of the models including presence status as driver had short stay as the variable which
contributed to the highest probability of a window opening. The presence status which contributed
to the lowest probability of window opening was intermediate for most of the models. This was
also unexpected, as it was presumed that departing would be the presence status contributing to
the lowest probabilities of window openings. These distributions could be due to the fact that
the presence algorithm was not constructed to handle several people in the residence or take into
account that the main door was moved without people leaving the resident. Even though a person
departed, there could still be another occupant at home.
Lastly, figure 65 indicates that the room type with the highest probability of a window opening
differed much from dwelling to dwelling. Most models had bedroom as the room type contributing
to the highest probabilities.
It was also of interest to see which of the included explanatory variables had the largest influence
on the window opening probability, i.e. which variable changed the slope/intercept the most. A
comparison of the influence of included variables was therefore carried out in the following way:
• Continuous explanatory variables: The coefficient of each included continuous variable
was multiplied with the respective measured data. Considering CO2 as an example, the de-
rived CO2-coefficient was multiplied with the absolute range of the measured CO2-conditions
in the respective dwelling; βCO2*(|log(CO2max+1)-log(CO2min+1)|)
• Categorical explanatory variables: The largest absolute difference between the inter-
cepts of each included categorical variable were equal to the influence of the respective vari-
able. Considering season as an example, the differences between the intercepts for spring,
summer, fall, and winter were found and the largest difference was the largest influence of
99 of 253
==PAGE:114==
Chapter 6 | Results
this categorical variable.
• Interaction terms: The coefficients of each included interaction term were multiplied with
the respective measured data as for the continuous variables. The largest of these was equal
to the influence from the included interaction term.
Based on this procedure, figure 66 presents the ranking of the explanatory variables which were
included in the 35 derived window opening models. The colored ranking is given in percentage of
the total amount of window opening models.
Figure 66: Ranked influence of included explanatory variables in the window opening models
As seen in figure 66, maximum 12 different explanatory variables were included in the window
opening models. Both in- and outdoor temperature were the most included continuous variables in
the window opening models and their influence on window opening probabilities were also found
to be high. 21% of all opening models had indoor temperature as the most influential driver.
Additionally, 12% of the models had outdoor temperature as the most influential driver. Figure
66 reveals that indoor temperature was ranked to be the first to fourth most influential driver
for window openings when it was included as explanatory variable. Also, indoor RH was ranked
2-5 in the models including this parameter which was odd since human beings do not sense RH
in the same way as the other continuous explanatory variables. An explanation could be 1) the
collinearity between temperature and RH or 2) airing out after activities leading to higher RHs
100 of 253
==PAGE:115==
6.5. Final models
as cooking, showering, etc. The influence of RH will be further discussed in 7 Discussion.
In regards to the categorical variables which were the most common variables in the derived
window opening models, the ranking varied greatly across these models. Season was the most
included variable in the window opening models and figure 66 shows that 40% of all window
opening models had season as the most influential driver. Hereby, it can be concluded that season,
across all models, was the most influential driver. In addition, minimum 45% of the models had
categorical variables in the upper half of the ranking system.
Therefore, even though the time related and contextual variables were most often included in the
window opening models, they were not necessarily the explanatory variable having the largest
influence on the probability. Thus, the most influential window opening drivers were 1) season,
2) indoor temperature, and 3) outdoor temperature.

the reasons behind were similar to the window opening models, as presumed.

this closing would also appear in the presence status arriving. The explanation could also be that
many occupants were at home for longer periods and that the windows not necessarily stood open
until the occupant left again. Otherwise, the reason could be the way the presence algorithm was
constructed.

variable in 6% of all the models.
Thus, the most influential window closing drivers were 1) indoor temperature and season, 2)
