7
Discussion
This chapter will discuss the choices made during the experimental setup, data handling and
modelling as well as the sources of errors. Furthermore, suggestions of topics for further work will
be presented.
7.1
Measuring equipment
This section will discuss the measuring equipment which was installed in the dwellings during the
research period. There were many problems and error sources related to the measuring equipment
which will be mentioned here.
Generally, the wireless sensor tags were extremely unstable. It was noticed during visits in the
dwellings that the Reed sensors did not always detect door movements. If a detection was missing
it could have had an affect on the presence algorithm. Contrary, the PIR sensors were quite stable
and functioned as desired. However, the wireless sensor tags with the largest error margins were
without a doubt the compass tags. There were several issues connected to the compass tags.
First of all, setting the sensitivities of the compass tags was extremely difficult as high sensitives
109 of 253
==PAGE:124==
Chapter 7 | Discussion
led to too many observations and low sensitivities led to missing observations. In order to get
the correct x-, y-, and z-coordinates from a window movement, it also required a fast, smooth,
and rapid movement of the window. This was most likely not the case when the occupants in
dwellings moved their windows themselves. Slower and more choppy movements led to misleading
measurements, as detections could occur before the window was fully opened or closed. This
meant that in the data, it would e.g. seem as if the window simply was moved from one opening
angle to another, and thus, the closing was missed. Also, in relation to sensitivity, it was noticed
during the experiment that if the battery level of the tag was too low, the tag would automatically
adjust the sensitivity to 50%. For nearly all tags, this sensitivity level was way too low to register
openings and closings. Therefore, there were fewer measurements during spring and summer than
fall and winter since Tasnim Azzouz [38], Anne Sloth Bidstrup [5], and Rune Korsholm Andersen
[1] started the experiment during winter 2016-2017, and the authors of this thesis continued the
experiment in fall 2017.
Secondly, there was no coherence between the compass tags’ x-, y- and z-readings which was
why it was necessary to create individual calibration regressions per tag. Since the calibrations
were performed manually, they were subject to mistaken observations and therefore misleading
calibrations. Even when the calibrations were made, the calibrations were only valid for a couple
of weeks for most tags since the reading coordinates fluctuated over time. This may have been due
to lowering battery voltages or fluctuating tag temperatures. Nevertheless, no reason behind the
fluctuations was found, hence the compass tag measurements were just extremely unstable. The
manual adjustment of opening angles was also an error source. It was a quite drastic manipulation
of data which included many underlying assumptions. E.g. if the opening angle was below 4◦, the
window detection was assumed to be a window closing. This could have been the case for many
detections, but not for all.
Thirdly, the placement of the tags was not optimal, and it would be recommended to move them
if the research project should be repeated in the future. Many tags were placed on the windows
with velcro so that the occupants could remove them e.g. when cleaning their windows. If the
tags afterwards were not set up exactly in the place they were before, the calibrations would not
work as the coordinates then would change drastically. Also, the tags were placed on the window
glass instead of the window frame. This was problematic since cold temperatures decreased the
battery life, and also because the condensation on the windows damaged the tags making them
even more unstable than they already were. Some tags were also placed near the window hinge
which meant that the movement of the tag was very limited. The coordinates of the compass tag
and hence the angles were not affected by this. However, detections were more likely to be missed
when they were placed near the hinge due to the limited movement. In order for these tags to
detect measurements, it was necessary to increase the sensitivities drastically. Due to these issues,
it would be recommended in another experiment to place the tags 1) in the corner furthest away
from the hinge where the movement of the window is the largest, 2) on the window frame instead
of the glass, and 3) on the top of the window due to condensation .
110 of 253
==PAGE:125==
7.2. Participants
Lastly, some compass tags lost their associations to their tag manager during the experiment
which meant that they stopped loading their data to the online URL. This again led to missing
observations for window openings and closings.
Contrary to the wireless sensor tags, the indoor Netatmo modules were much more stable. They
recorded continuously without big problems. Only few issues were evaluated as error sources/
limitations.
One issue was that if the main Netatmo indoor module was not connected to a
power slot for a longer period than the offline capacity period, none of the modules would record
observations. The largest issue with the indoor Netatmos was, however, the continuous calibration
in regards to indoor CO2, cf. 5.2.2.1 Indoor climate. When looking at CO2-measurements in figure
47, the low CO2 levels indicated that the calibration was performed when it probably should not
have been. This resulted in window openings being measured during lower CO2-levels than the
case was in reality. In future research, the accuracy of the CO2 could be improved by asking the
participants to bring the Netatmo modules outside so that they reach measurements at 400ppm
[31].
In regards to the outdoor Netatmo measuring equipment, everything functioned reasonably. The
only equipment which could have been installed more efficiently was the wind gauge. In order
to get correct measurements, the wind gauge should have been installed on a bare field without
surrounding obstacles. This was not possible in this study and should therefore be seen as a
limitation.
7.2
Participants
As already mentioned, human behaviour differs widely between human beings. Therefore, when
creating a model representing human behaviour, it should be taken into account whether the
participants cover a broad spectrum of different kinds of people.
This is especially necessary
if the model is intended to be used on datasets based in different scenarios than the reference
scenario.
It was known to the researchers in this research project that the type of occupants in the par-
ticipating dwellings varied between different generations, different constellations of people living
in the dwellings; singles, couples, families as well as people with different social backgrounds.
However, a specific categorization of occupant types was not included in the model. By including
this, the models might reveal a more specific pattern for one type of occupants than others. This
could e.g. be used when estimating energy consumption of dwellings built for a specific group of
occupants; families, retired people, etc.
When speaking of different types of occupants, the question on whether this relative small excerpt
of the Danish population generated a general picture of how Danes interact with windows in their
111 of 253
==PAGE:126==
Chapter 7 | Discussion
private dwellings arose. The 2nd of December 2017, Ph.D. student Verena M. Barthelmes from
Polytechnic University of Turin, Senior Researcher Henrik N. Knudsen from Aalborg University,
and Rune Korsholm Andersen handed out questionnaires to the participants behind this research
project. The questionnaires were filled out during December 2017. Among others, the question-
naires included a time use survey which revealed how the participants spent their time. The result
of this is illustrated in figure 73.
Figure 73: Time use survey on participants behind this research project. Adapted from [3]
Additionally, a time use survey based on 17,707 people in Denmark was performed in 2008/2009
[4]. The result from this survey is illustrated in figure 74.
112 of 253
==PAGE:127==
7.2. Participants
Figure 74: General Danish population time use based on 17,707 Danes in 2008/2009. From [4]
Based on the two time use surveys in figure 73 and 74, it is seen that the participants in this
project had similar time use patterns as a larger group of the Danish population. The shape of
the curves in each figure show the same tendencies of time use during an entire day where e.g. the
eating category was larger during noon and dinner time than the rest of the day. Additionally,
people in this project tended to go to bed, wake up, and not being home in similar time frames and
amount as the larger part of the population. These time use surveys hereby supported that the
small excerpt of Danes participating in this project covered a general time use pattern for Danes.
This did, however, not mean that their window operation models covered a general behaviour
pattern for Danes; it only revealed that similarities between the participants and the general
Danish population existed.
As stated in 4 Modelling strategy, the derived models in this master thesis only included be-
havioural drivers related to the environment, time, and context of the room. Hence, other factors
influencing the behaviour such as the human physic, psyche, and social life as well as random
factors were not included. Additionally, several other contextual factors such as knowledge about
the building, its installations, and the surrounding area were also not included.
By incorpo-
rating these drivers, other explanatory variables might show a great influence on the occupant
behaviour. Human beings are e.g. sensitive towards change in odor which could be a driver for
opening windows.
113 of 253
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 7 | Discussion
Another factor that might influence the occupant behaviour was the fact that the participants were
aware that they were being monitored and included in an experiment. Additionally, the occupants
were aware of the overall purpose of the experiment and could therefore be biased. This might
drive some people to act differently in order to act as ”correct” as possible, also known as the
Hawthorne effect [47]. However, it could be argued that in experiments during a longer period as
e.g. one year, the participants’ focus on being a part of an experiment eventually fades.
Generally in this research project, many participants revealed that problems with mould and mois-
ture occurred in their dwellings. Therefore, it was of high interest for many of the participants
to get an insight in how their indoor environment was. Hence, occupants agreeing to partici-
pate in the experiment might belong to a certain type of occupants who were interested in the
indoor environment and wanted to improve their own living conditions. Furthermore, it should
be mentioned that the occupant behaviour in this research project could have been influenced
by the feedback given in the time frame of Anne Sloth Bidstrups master thesis. Here, a group
of occupants were given weekly feedback on their indoor environment including specific advice of
how to act differently in order to improve the indoor environment. The feedback was given during
a five week period up until May 2017 [5]. Additionally, since May 2017, all participants had access
to measurements of their indoor environment through my.netatmo.com. This could also influence
their behaviour since the availability to follow the indoor climate development might make people
more aware of different parameters than usual. Also, since the occupants lived in a close commu-
nity, the occupants receiving feedback might be discussing their advises with the other occupants
and hereby influence their behaviour. However, since the feedback period was relatively short
compared to the length of the experiment, some occupants might also fall back into their old
behavioural patterns. To investigate whether the measurements of indoor temperature, indoor
RH, and indoor CO2-concentration differed between the feedback group and reference group in the
time frame after Anne Sloth Bidstrups feedback period, boxplots were made. Boxplots illustrating
measurements for all feedback dwellings and all reference dwellings are presented in figure 75, 76,
and 77.
114 of 253
==PAGE:129==
7.3. Assumptions
Figure 75: Indoor temperature
measurements after feedback
period, feedback group vs.
reference group
Figure 76: Indoor RH
measurements after feedback
period, feedback group vs.
reference group
Figure 77: Indoor
CO2-measurements after
feedback period, feedback group
vs. reference group
Figure 75, 76, and 77 show that the difference in measurements between the feedback group and the
reference group was not remarkable. The reference group had slightly lower indoor temperature
and indoor CO2 measurements than the feedback group. In regards to indoor RH, the reference
group generally had higher values. However, the differences were minimal and therefore indicated
that the occupants’ behaviour was not remarkably affected by the feedback after the feedback
period ended.
Lastly, it should be kept in mind that all dwellings in this research project were townhouses from
1981 which had not undergone a renovation with implementation of new technologies. Therefore,
behavioural patterns modelled in this thesis might not be applicable on e.g. high technological
buildings since occupant behaviour tend to change with building type [34].
7.3
Assumptions
Throughout the data handling and modelling of window operations, several assumptions were
made.
How these assumptions could have affected the final models will be discussed in this
section.
A limitation in this research project was that measurements on window movements were carried
out in rooms without measured indoor climate. Compass tags in these rooms were therefore linked
115 of 253
==PAGE:130==
Chapter 7 | Discussion
to average indoor climate of measurements in additional rooms. Also the CO2-concentrations in
rooms with an outdoor module were assumed to be equal to an average of the other measurements.
These assumptions had the risk of influencing the models wrongly if the indoor climate varied
a lot between the measured and non-measured rooms. The difference between indoor climate
measurements from Netatmo modules was therefore investigated in the dwellings. As an example,
figure 78 and 79 presents the measurement differences between the modules in dwelling 1 and 21,
respectively. The calculated differences are according to the main indoor module. The following
abbreviations are used in the figures: MI = main indoor module, AI = additional indoor module,
and O = outdoor module.
(a) Temperature
(b) RH
(c) CO2-concentration
Figure 78: Example of differences in indoor climate measurements between rooms, dwelling 1
116 of 253
==PAGE:131==
7.3. Assumptions
(a) Temperature
(b) RH
(c) CO2-concentration
Figure 79: Example of differences in indoor climate measurements between rooms, dwelling 21
Figure 78 and 79 show that differences in temperature mainly were between 0-4.2◦C but also
reached 6-7◦C. The indoor RH measurements had the majority of differences between 0-10% but
also reached differences around 20%.
Additionally, the measured CO2-concentrations differed
between 0-430ppm and 0-575ppm for dwelling 1 and 21, respectively. The difference in measured
CO2 even reached 3000ppm and a lot of outliers were seen. Thus, the indoor climate at some points
varied remarkably from room to room which could have led to unreliable data when the assumption
of average indoor climate data was made. Some of the compass tags in the non-measured rooms
were positioned much closer to one Netatmo module than another. Hence, the indoor climate
linked to the window movements might be more realistic if data from the closest module was used
instead, especially if the rooms were in open relation with each other. Nonetheless, distributing
these compass tags to existing Netatmo modules would 1) still not give correct indoor climate
for the rooms and 2) include a more extensive workload. It was therefore considered reasonable
to combine all these compass tags to average data since none of the solutions included the exact
indoor climate conditions for the respective rooms. Otherwise, additional Netatmo modules in
these rooms could remedy this problem. However, this would require more copying of data which
also will influence the models. Furthermore, the copying of data to fill in small data gaps could
also have led to wrong estimates.
117 of 253
==PAGE:132==
Chapter 7 | Discussion
In the final data tables on which the models were derived, offline periods for the wireless sensor
tags were not excluded. If only a single tag was offline, it was not possible to delete the period
without deleting the same period for other tags. Due to instability of the compass tags, a high
risk of deleting large amount of useful data from other tasks then appeared. Hence, offline periods
for single tags were not deleted. However, if the entire wireless tag system was offline, it would
have been preferable to delete the period so that longer periods without possible window opening
measurements did not influence the probability in the final models.
Since the duration of some window openings were extremely high, it was questionable whether a
more realistic dataset would appear if the above was taken into account. It could e.g. be assumed
that when an opening duration exceeded 24 hours, a window closing was most likely missed
and the window status would be changed from open to closed. However, this would eliminate
situations where e.g. bedroom windows on the first floor were open during longer periods. Since
the behaviour was different from dwelling to dwelling, it was considered best not to manipulate
the data in this case.
During the data handling process, manipulation of data was required in order to use data from
the compass tags; angle calibration regressions were applied and hereafter manually adjusted due
to large angle fluctuations.
Thus, some opening angles in the data might differ from reality.
Additionally, large opening durations could also be due to incorrect adjustment of angles.
Another important assumption was that multiple windows in one room were modelled as one
combined window. This limitation made it impossible to distinguish between individual windows
but was considered necessary in order to minimize the risk of overrepresenting data. Despite this
modelling choice, overrepresentation of data could still appear as time-related, outdoor climate,
and contextual variables were inserted up to four times depending on the dwelling. This might
be the reason why the most included explanatory variables for all model types were found to be
some of the time-related variables and room type.
Furthermore, the presence algorithm did not distinguish between the number of occupants being
present. The presence status arriving could therefore easily occur when a person being at home
opened and closed the door without leaving the house. Having this scenario where presence status
arriving was applied without the person actually arriving could cause inaccurate window opening
probabilities. Hence, the risk of presence status appearing less important than it actually was
eventuated.
In this master thesis, window movements were not included in the presence algorithm. It was found
that 30% of all window movements occurred when no occupants were present. However, 92% of
these window movements occurred during fall where the sensitivity for a part of this period was
too high. Additionally, 79% of the movements happening when no presence was estimated during
fall were closings, which frequently occurred for closed windows with high sensitivity settings.
118 of 253
==PAGE:133==
7.4. Final models
Therefore, the presence algorithm was considered reliable without the window movements.
When deriving the models it was considered which interaction terms were most important to
include in the model. As stated in 5.4 Modelling of window operation, interaction terms were
only included for presence status vs. continuous variables. However, other scenarios might also
be crucial as well; e.g. indoor temperature having different impact on window operations in room
types bedroom and livingroom. It could therefore be interesting to see whether other interaction
terms were of higher influence than the ones modelled in this thesis.
When opening a window, the chosen opening angle might also depend on the window size and
type. Generally, larger windows might be more likely to be opened in smaller angles than small
windows. Additionally, not all windows in this experiment had the possibility of opening more
than 45◦. By including window type as an explanatory variable, the influence of window type on
the opening angle would also be investigated.
Lastly, and very importantly, one of the main assumptions in the modelling of window operations
was that a linear coherence between the explanatory variables and window movements existed.
Since the residual plots revealed that this assumption was violated for all the window opening
and window closing models, the reliability of the models was questionable.
7.4
Final models
A general tendency found in the final models was that the window opening models included more
explanatory variables than the window closing and opening angle models. Opening angle model
was the model type including least explanatory variables. This could be due to the window opening
models generally being derived from larger datasets than the other two model types.
In order to get an overview of whether the most included explanatory variables also were the most
influential, table 18 lists the top three for all model types.
Table 18: Most included vs. most influential explanatory variables in the models
Model type
Most included variables
Most influential variables
Window opening
1) Season
1) Season
2) Room type
2) Indoor temperature
3) Time of day
3) Outdoor temperature
Window closing
1) Room type
1) Indoor temperature and season
2) Season
2) Indoor RH
3) Indoor RH
3) Outdoor temperature
Opening angle
1) Room type
1) Room type
2) Season
2) Outdoor temperature
3) Outdoor temperature
3) Season
119 of 253
==PAGE:134==
Chapter 7 | Discussion
As table 18 lists, season was both a part of the top three most included and most influential
explanatory variables. This indicated that season was of high influence towards window operations
in this research project. However, as already mentioned, some of the dwellings were offline during
parts of summer and this season was therefore underrepresented in the models. This could also
be the reason why season was included and ranked as influential predictor in so many models,
since the variation between the different season measurements was high. Additionally, indoor and
outdoor temperature were found to be some of the most influential variables even though they
were not the most included variables in the models.
Many of the most included variables were categorical variables which were copied during the
transformation of the data tables. Their high inclusion could therefore have been affected by the
overrepresentation of data.
Presence status was included as explanatory variable in many of the window opening and closing
models and was also of relatively high influence.
However, there were odd results in regards
to the probability of when an opening and closing was highest.
The results could have been
influenced by the way the presence status algorithm was formed. Therefore, window operations
could theoretically occur equally in all presence status’ and give misleading results. To remedy
this problem in the future, it would be necessary to know the number of occupants and be able to
distinguish whether e.g. occupant 1 or 2 left the house. This could be done by having monitors
on the occupants or by including a time survey per occupant in the algorithm.
Furthermore, indoor RH was frequently included in all model types and was also found to have
a high influence on the probability in the models where it was included. Even though human
beings do not obviously feel the RH of the surrounding air, it was not surprising that it was
included in many models since 1) there is coherence between RH and activities and 2) there is
close relation between RH and temperature. The second explanation supports the fact that the
assumption of collinearity was violated in the modelling of window operations since RH might have
been included in the models even though temperature was the actual driver behind the action.
This was investigated by taking all the models including in- and/or outdoor RH, changing this
explanatory variable to the respective temperature, deriving the models again, and comparing AIC
between the old and new model. The results showed that only the window opening and closing
model of dwelling 20 had lower AICs when outdoor RH was replaced with outdoor temperature.
The reason why the models with the lowest AIC were not chosen to begin with was that the
stepwise forward and backward selection in R only found the local minimum and not the global
minimum. Therefore, R did not try out the variable combination which led to the lower AICs of
the models for dwelling 20. Since only 4% of the models (all model types) including in- and/or
outdoor RH had a smaller AIC when exchanging RH with temperature, it indicated that RH
better explained the variance than temperature.
When comparing the derived models with the general findings in previous research studies, it was
120 of 253
==PAGE:135==
7.5. Further work
seen that many other studies also found season to be of high influence on window operations.
Additionally, they also stated that the window opening probability was highest during mornings
and evenings. This was also similar to this thesis where morning and afternoon had the highest
window opening probability and morning and evening had the highest window closing probability.
Similar to many previous studies which evaluated in- and outdoor temperatures as the most
important drivers, in- and/or outdoor temperature were also found in the top three influential
drivers for all model types in this thesis, see table 18. Contrary to these similarities, the previously
mentioned research in Switzerland found that larger opening angles occurred during winter than
in summer. In this thesis, opening angles were generally largest during summer and spring.
In the reflection of the final models derived in this master thesis, it was also questioned whether
the models were reliable or not.
Even though many assumptions were made throughout the
modelling, the models were still based on actual data. Today, when predicting energy consumption
of buildings, models representing occupant behaviour are solely based on guesswork [1].
The
question is therefore; will the models based on actual data perform better than the ones based on
guesswork? Here, the answer will most likely be; yes. However, modelling of occupant behaviour
is complex and when looking at the models derived in this master thesis, an extensive need of
improvements and further development is still required. Nonetheless, this modelling of window
operations in residential buildings is still a step in the right direction towards understanding
occupant behaviour and receiving more reliable estimations of energy performance.
7.5
Further work
There are several issues which could be interesting to investigate in further research. This section
will mention the issues which potentially could improve the models of this thesis, and it will give
suggestions of which other perspectives could be looked into.
Since the residual plots for the window opening and closing models did not indicate linearity,
it would be interesting to see if an extra variable could help the model fits.
An explanatory
variable which possibly could have an effect on the window action probabilities could be day
type (weekday vs. weekend) since it is presumable that the probability for opening and closing
windows is different in the weekends than on weekdays. In further research, other explanatory
variables of more sociological character could also be taken into account and perhaps improve
the linearity; age, gender, BMI, smoker/non-smoker, etc. This could be included by using the
answers to the questionnaires which were handed out to the participants. The answers to the
questionnaires were received in the last part of the thesis period, which is why it was possible
to include them. Additionally, more contextual parameters and e.g. window types could also be
added as explanatory variables.
Another way to improve the model fit could be investigating whether other statistical meth-
121 of 253
==PAGE:136==
Chapter 7 | Discussion
ods better describe the data than GLM with logistic regression. Other methods could e.g. be
transformed polynomial models, transformed exponential models, transformed power models, etc.
Theoretically, there are no limitations of what could be tried out. Practically, however, there are
limitations of which functions R can handle [1].
Validation of the models could also be an interesting topic in further research. This could e.g.
be done by implementing them in a simulation program and checking whether the results were
realistic. Towards the end of this thesis period, the research project also obtained access approval
to the energy and heating usage documentation of the dwellings. When the documents are re-
ceived, simulation results could, thus, be compared with the actual usage to figure out whether
the results were realistic. The questionnaires to the participants could also be used to validate the
assumptions behind the models. The questions asking about time use could e.g. either validate
or invalidate the assumptions regarding occupant presence.
This experiment was set up to measure behaviour in an entire dwelling and, hence, occupant
behaviour was modelled per household and not per individual person. It could be interesting to
see whether the models based on an entire dwelling would predict different behaviour than models
based on individuals. When speaking of diversity between occupants, the optimal way to include
this would be to model the occupants individually since people living in the same resident still
act differently.
In this thesis, occupant diversity was included by making separate models per dwelling. A mixed
effects model could in future research be made and compared to the models of this thesis. This
would be interesting in order to determine how diversity best is implemented in occupant be-
havioural models.
122 of 253
==PAGE:137==
8
Conclusion
The aim of this master thesis was to model occupant behaviour stochastically in relation to
window operations in residential buildings by using measured data from a field study.
Three
model types were derived per dwelling; two generalized linear models predicting window opening
probability and window closing probability, respectively, and a linear model predicting the opening
angle.
The measurements were carried out in a living compound north of Copenhagen where 37 dwellings
were monitored in regards to indoor climate, window operations, and presence in the dwellings.
Additionally, outdoor climate was measured next to the housing area.
In total, this resulted in 35 window opening models, 34 window closing models, and 33 opening
angle models describing the probability of an action happening in the respective dwelling and how
large the opening angle then would be. The models covered 9-12 months of measured behaviour
and a total number of 179 windows located in different room types. The models were intended
to be used in simulation software as an indicator of how occupants interact with windows in
residential buildings. Diversity was included by separately modelling behavioural patterns per
dwelling.
123 of 253
==PAGE:138==
| Conclusion
The measurements showed that the indoor climate differed from dwelling to dwelling.
Both
indoor temperature and indoor RH was found to be within acceptable levels.
In regards to
CO2-concentrations, unrealistically low measurements occurred which had the risk of influencing
the modelled probabilities wrongly. By comparing the outdoor climate measurements with DMI
measurements, it was found that the measured outdoor data was reliable.
The presence algorithm explained the amount of occupied hours properly. However, implementa-
tion of number of occupants would improve the presence status accuracy.
The measured data on windows revealed a correlation between the opening angles and opening
durations; the larger the opening angle, the smaller the opening duration. The tendency between
the two parameters seemed exponential. Additionally, it was found that the opening angle and
opening duration varied over season and room type.
The models showed a difference in included explanatory variables across both model type and
dwelling. Generally, the window opening models contained most explanatory variables whereas
the opening angle models contained fewest. A common pattern across all models was that the
most included variables were season and room type.
However, the most influential variables
varied between model types with 1) season, indoor temperature, and outdoor temperature in the
window opening models, 2) indoor temperature and season, indoor relative humidity, and outdoor
temperature in the window closing models, and 3) room type, outdoor temperature, and season
in the opening angle models.
Since the models were based on actual data, they might improve the accuracy of energy simulations
of residential buildings. However, model validations and improvements are still needed in order
to enhance the quality of the predictions. Nevertheless, it can be concluded that the modelling of
occupant behaviour is a step in the right direction. In addition, the process in this master thesis
could be used as a guideline for similar research projects.

==PAGE:161==
D
Opening angles
In this appendix, an overview of how the interval of opening angles were distributed per room
type in each dwelling will be presented.
Figure D.1 and D.2 illustrate the interval of the measured opening angles for dwelling 1-17 and
18-33, respectively. The angle interval covers all windows in the respective rooms. Measurements
from dwelling 34 and 35 are not included since opening angle models were not derived for these
dwellings due to too small data sets.
147 of 253
==PAGE:162==
Appendix D | Opening angles
Figure D.1: Opening angle interval per room type and dwelling, dwelling 1-17
148 of 253
==PAGE:163==
Figure D.2: Opening angle interval per room type and dwelling, dwelling 18-33
When looking at figure D.1 and D.2, it is seen that the minimum opening angles in 92 out of the
total 100 rooms were smaller than 10◦. Actually, most of them were around 4-6◦which were
smaller than the crack opening hinges on the windows. The small opening angles could e.g. be
due to high sensitivity or discontinuous opening movements resulting in measurements before an
actual opening or closing was completed. However, seven of the investigated rooms with different
room types had minimum opening angles between 18◦and 36◦showing that crack openings were
not performed on all windows. Figure D.1 and D.2 also reveal that opening angles reaching 90◦
or above happened in 34 rooms varying between all room types.
149 of 253
==PAGE:164==
Appendix D | Opening angles
150 of 253
